Sawm — Fasting
beginner
6 min
Definition and Obligation of Fasting (Sawm)
Defines fasting, its cause, and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fasting and list the four conditions that make fasting Ramadan obligatory.

Core Idea
Fasting is abstaining from inserting anything and from sexual intercourse, with intention, during daytime.

Fasting is a central act of worship in Islam. The Hanafi school defines it precisely to remove ambiguity. This passage from Nur al-Idah gives the foundational definition, identifies the cause that triggers the obligation of Ramadan fasting, and lists the essential conditions (shurut) that must be present for the fast to be obligatory. Understanding these elements is the first step to mastering the fiqh of sawm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components of imsak
The definition states that fasting is abstaining (imsak) from two things: (1) inserting anything into the belly or what has the ruling of the interior, and (2) sexual desire (intercourse). Note that both are mentioned separately, and even mistaken insertion breaks the fast.
The word 'batnan' refers to the internal cavity; the phrase 'ma lahu hukm al-batin' extends to areas like the eye or ear if something enters, but that is detailed later.
2
Extract the cause (sabab) for Ramadan fasting
The passage states: 'Wa sabab wujub Ramadan shuhud juz'' – the cause for the obligation of fasting Ramadan is witnessing a part of the crescent moon. Each day of Ramadan is a cause for performing that day's fast.
This means the obligation is not automatic by the mere arrival of the month; it requires actual sighting or completion of Sha'ban.
3
List the four conditions (shurut) for obligation
The passage says fasting is fard upon one who has four things: Islam, sanity ('aql), puberty (bulugh), and knowledge of the obligation for someone who converts in Dar al-Harb (or being in Dar al-Islam, where ignorance is usually not an excuse).
The text cuts off, but the fourth condition is explained in commentaries: knowledge of the obligation for a new Muslim in a land where Islamic knowledge is not available, so they are not held accountable until they learn.

Source Text

١ـ وسبب وجوب رمضان شهود جزء
وكل يوم منه : سبب لأدائه (٢).
حكمه، وشروط فرضيته :]
وهو فرض، أداءً وقضاء، على من اجتمع فيه أربعة أشياء:
۱ - الإسلام.
الإسلام".
٢- والعقل.
- والبلوغ.

Book of Fasting
It is abstention during the daytime:
1- From intentionally or mistakenly inserting anything into the belly or what has the ruling of the interior.
2- And from the desire of the private part (sexual intercourse), with intention from its people.
[The cause of the obligation of fasting:]
1- And the cause of the obligation of Ramadan is witnessing a part (of the crescent).
And every day of it is a cause for its performance.
[Its ruling, and conditions of its obligation:]
And it is an obligation, both performance and making up, upon the one in whom four things are present:
1- Islam.
2- Sanity.
- Puberty.
And knowledge of the obligation for one who embraces Islam in Dar al-Harb, or being in Dar...
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Sawm (Book of Fasting) begins with the definition: fasting is abstaining (imsak) during the daytime from two things: (1) inserting anything into the belly (internal cavity) intentionally or mistakenly, including what is considered internal like the brain; and (2) sexual desire (i.e., sexual intercourse), all with intention from a person qualified to fast. The cause (sabab) for the obligation of Ramadan is the sighting (shuhud) of part of the crescent moon; each day of Ramadan is a cause for performing that day's fast. The ruling is that fasting is fard (obligatory) both as performance (ada') and making up (qada') upon one who meets four conditions: Islam, sanity, puberty, and knowledge of the obligation for someone who accepts Islam in Dar al-Harb or being in Dar al-Islam (presumably no excuse for ignorance). The text here cuts off but establishes the key principles.

Key Terms
الإمساك (al-imsak): Abstention from nullifiers of fasting from dawn to sunset with intention.
سبب وجوب (sabab wujub): The cause that makes the act obligatory. For Ramadan, it is sighting the crescent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the cause (sabab) for the obligation of fasting Ramadan?
A) The arrival of the month of Sha'ban
B) Witnessing a part of the crescent (shuhud juz')
C) The age of puberty (bulugh)
D) Embracing Islam

Key Takeaway
Fasting Ramadan is obligatory upon sighting the crescent; its definition includes abstention from two specific categories, and four conditions must be met for the obligation to apply.

Sawm — Fasting

Basic Rulings of Fasting in Ramadan
Defines fasting and lists its conditions and rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fasting, identify the conditions for its obligation and validity, and explain the pillar of fasting.

Core Idea
Fasting is intentional abstention from the two desires with intention; its pillar is the abstention itself.

This passage from Nur al-Idah opens the Book of Fasting with a concise definition and the essential legal rulings. It distinguishes between the conditions that make fasting obligatory and those that make it valid, and clarifies that the very act of abstention is the pillar of fasting. Understanding these foundational elements is crucial before studying the detailed rules of what invalidates the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and cause
Read the first lines: fasting is abstaining during the daytime from two desires with intention. Note that the cause (sabab) for obligation is witnessing the new moon of Ramadan.
Each day of Ramadan is a separate cause for that day's fast.
2
Distinguish conditions for obligation vs. validity
The passage lists conditions for wujub al-ada (obligation to perform) and for sihhat al-ada (validity of performance). The former include health and residence; the latter include intention, freedom from menstruation/postpartum bleeding, and freedom from invalidating acts.
Janabah (major impurity) is NOT a condition for validity - a key Hanafi ruling.
3
Identify the pillar (rukn)
The rukn is specifically the abstention (kuff) from the two desires of belly and private part. Intention is a condition (shart), not the pillar.
This distinguishes Hanafi from some other schools that consider intention as rukn.
4
Note the ruling (hukm)
Fasting Ramadan is fard (obligatory) on every Muslim who meets the four conditions: Islam, sanity, puberty, and knowledge of obligation. The reward is in the Hereafter.
This is a firm ruling, muttafaq alayh across schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم
هو الإمساك نهاراً:
كتاب الصوم

٢- وعن شهوة الفرج. بنية من أهله.
ا سبب وجوب الصوم :]
١ـ وسبب وجوب رمضان شهود جزء
وكل يوم منه : سبب لأدائه (٢).
حكمه، وشروط فرضيته :]
وهو فرض، أداءً وقضاء، على من اجتمع فيه أربعة أشياء:
۱ - الإسلام.
الإسلام".
٢- والعقل.
- والبلوغ.
والعلم بالوجوب لمن أسلم بدار الحرب، أو الكون بدار
(۱) وهو الدماغ، كدواء الآمة. مراقي.
(٢) أي سبب لوجوب أدائه. مراقي. (۳) فلا عُذر له بالجهل.

شروط وجوب أدائه : ]
ويُشترط لوجوب أدائه :
۱ ـ الصحة من مرض، وحيض، ونفاس.
٢- والإقامة.
شروط صحة أدائه :]
ويُشترط لصحة أدائه ثلاثة:
١ - النية. ۲- والخُلُو عما يُنافيه من حيض، ونفاس.
٣- وعما يُفسده.
ولا يُشترط الخُلُو عن الجنابة.
[ ركن الصوم : ]
وركنه : الكف عن قضاء شهوتي البطن، والفرج، وما أُلحق بهما.
[حكم من صامه :]
وحكمه: سقوط الواجب عن الذمة، والثواب في الآخرة.
Book of Fasting. It is abstaining during the daytime: 1. From intentionally or mistakenly introducing something into the belly or what has the ruling of the belly. 2. And from the desire of the private part, with intention from its people. The cause of obligation of fasting: The cause of obligation of Ramadan is witnessing a part (of the month), and each day of it is a cause for its performance. Its ruling and conditions of its being fard: It is fard, performance and make-up, upon the one in whom four things are combined: 1. Islam, 2. Sanity, 3. Puberty, 4. Knowledge of obligation for one who accepts Islam in dar al-harb or is in dar al-harb (so he has no excuse of ignorance). Conditions for its obligation of performance: It is required for its obligation of performance: 1. Health from illness, menstruation, and postpartum bleeding. 2. Residence. Conditions for validity of its performance: It is required for validity of its performance three things: 1. Intention, 2. Being free from what contradicts it of menstruation and postpartum bleeding, 3. And from what invalidates it. Being free from major ritual impurity is not required. The pillar of fasting: Its pillar is refraining from fulfilling the two desires of the belly and the private part, and what is annexed to them. The ruling of one who fasts it: Its ruling is the obligation falling from the liability, and reward in the Hereafter.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah opens the Book of Fasting (Kitab al-Sawm) with a concise definition and the essential legal rulings. The author first defines fasting (sawm) as abstaining (imsak) during the daytime from two things: (1) introducing anything into the belly—whether intentionally or mistakenly, and whether directly or via a cavity that has the ruling of the belly—and (2) engaging in sexual desire (i.e., intercourse or related acts). The abstention must be accompanied by intention (niyyah). This definition is the standard Hanafi position: sawm is not merely abstention but abstention with intention. The cause (sabab) for the obligation of Ramadan fasting is the sighting of the new moon (shuhud juz' min Ramadan). Each day of the month becomes a separate cause for performing that day's fast. The ruling (hukm) of fasting Ramadan is that it is fard (obligatory) both as adā' (performing on time) and qadā' (making up if missed). This obligation applies to a person who meets four conditions: (1) Islam, (2) sanity, (3) puberty, and (4) knowledge of obligation—the last condition is primarily for someone who accepts Islam in dar al-harb (non-Muslim territory) or lives there, so that ignorance is not an excuse. After establishing the fardiyyah of fasting, the author distinguishes between conditions for the obligation of performance (shurut wujub al-ada) and conditions for validity (shurut sihhat al-ada). For the obligation to perform the fast, two conditions are needed: (a) being healthy (free from illness, menstruation, postpartum bleeding) and (b) being a resident (muqim) — a traveler is not obligated to fast during travel. For the validity of the fast, three conditions are required: (1) intention, (2) being free from menstruation and postpartum bleeding (since fasting is not valid in these states), and (3) being free from anything that invalidates the fast (e.g., eating, drinking, intercourse). Notably, being in a state of major ritual impurity (janabah) does NOT invalidate the fast—a person may have the intention to fast while in janabah. The pillar (rukn) of fasting is the actual abstention from fulfilling the two desires (shahwatay): the desire of the belly (food/drink) and the desire of the private part (sexual activity), along with anything legally annexed to them. The ruling (hukm) for one who performs the fast correctly is that the obligatory duty is discharged from his liability (dhimmah) and he is entitled to reward in the Hereafter. This is a straightforward presentation of the foundational rules of Ramadan fasting according to the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الصوم (as-sawm): Linguistically abstention; legally: intentional abstention from the two desires (belly and private part) from dawn to sunset.
الإمساك (al-imsak): Abstaining; in fasting, it refers to refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual activity.
ركن (rukn): The essential integral of fasting; without it, the act is invalid. Here: abstention from the two desires.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a condition for the validity (sihhah) of fasting according to the passage?
A) Being free from menstruation and postpartum bleeding.
B) Having the intention before dawn.
C) Being in a state of ritual purity (free from hadath akbar).
D) Being free from acts that invalidate the fast.

Key Takeaway
Fasting Ramadan is fard, with specific conditions for obligation and validity; the pillar is abstention with intention, and janabah does not invalidate.

Conditions, Pillar, and Ruling of Ramadan Fasting
Lists conditions, pillar, and ruling of Ramadan fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for obligation and validity of Ramadan fasting, identify its pillar, and explain the ruling.

Core Idea
Fasting is fard; validity requires intention and freedom from nullifiers; pillar is abstention.

This passage from Nur al-Idah lays the foundation for understanding Ramadan fasting. It distinguishes between conditions that make fasting obligatory and conditions that make it valid. The text also clearly defines the single pillar (rukn) and the overall ruling. Mastering these distinctions is essential before studying the detailed rules of Sawm.

Guided Path
1
Separate conditions for obligation vs validity
In the text, two sets of conditions are listed. Identify which conditions belong to wujub (obligation) and which to sihha (validity).
Obligation conditions: health and residence. Validity conditions: intention, freedom from mufsidat, and freedom from hayd/nifas.
2
State each condition with its category
Name each condition and state whether it is required for the fast to be obligatory or for the fast to be valid.
For example, 'health' is a condition for obligation; 'intention' is a condition for validity.
3
Define the pillar (rukn)
Extract the single pillar of fasting from the text: it is refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse, and what is legally analogous.
The rukn is the abstention itself, not the intention.
4
Explain the ruling (hukm)
What does performing the fast achieve? The text says it discharges the obligation (wajib) and brings reward in the Hereafter.
Note the term 'wajib' here is used generally; the Hanafi school considers it fard.
5
Note the exception: janabah
The text explicitly states that being in a state of major impurity (janabah) does not invalidate the fast.
This is a key distinction from state of prayer, where janabah is a preventer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم
شروط وجوب أدائه:
ويُشترط لوجوب أدائه:
1 - الصحة من مرض، وحيض، ونفاس.
2- والإقامة.
شروط صحة أدائه:
ويُشترط لصحة أدائه ثلاثة:
1 - النية. 2- والخُلُو عما يُنافيه من حيض، ونفاس.
3- وعما يُفسده.
ولا يُشترط الخُلُو عن الجنابة.
[ ركن الصوم : ]
وركنه : الكف عن قضاء شهوتي البطن، والفرج، وما أُلحق بهما.
[حكم من صامه :]
وحكمه: سقوط الواجب عن الذمة، والثواب في الآخرة.
The Book of Fasting. Conditions for its obligation to be performed: It is required for its obligation to be performed: 1. Health from illness, menstruation, and postpartum bleeding. 2. Residence. Conditions for the validity of its performance: Three conditions are required for its validity: 1. Intention. 2. Freedom from what contradicts it, such as menstruation and postpartum bleeding. 3. And from what nullifies it. Freedom from major ritual impurity is not required. The pillar of fasting: Its pillar is refraining from fulfilling the two desires of the stomach and private parts, and what is attached to them. The ruling for one who fasts it: Its ruling is the discharge of the obligation from the responsibility and reward in the Hereafter.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage sets out the foundational rulings on fasting in Ramadan. First, it lists the conditions that must be present for fasting to become obligatory: being healthy (not ill), being free from menstruation and postpartum bleeding, and being a resident (not traveling). These are preconditions that trigger the obligation if present. Next are conditions for the fast to be valid: having intention (niyyah), being free from anything that inherently contradicts the fast (like menstruation or postpartum bleeding during the day), and being free from anything that spoils it. Notably, being in a state of major impurity (janabah) does not invalidate the fast—one can fast while junub, though ghusl must be performed before prayer. The single pillar (rukn) of fasting is abstinence from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse, and anything legally equivalent. The ruling: the performer of the fast has discharged the obligatory duty and will receive reward in the Hereafter. These are essential points a beginner must memorize.

Key Terms
صحة (sihha): The condition of being free from illness that would make fasting harmful or extremely difficult, as a condition for obligation.
إقامة (iqaama): Being a resident (muqim), not a traveler, as a condition for obligation of fasting in Ramadan.
نية (niyya): Intention; a condition for validity of fasting. Must be made before dawn for Ramadan fasts.
ركن الصوم (rukn al-sawm): The pillar of fasting: abstaining from the two desires (eating/drinking and sexual activity) and what is legally analogous.
حكم الصوم (hukm al-sawm): The ruling: discharge of the obligatory duty and attainment of reward in the afterlife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a condition for the validity of fasting but NOT for its obligation?
A) Health from illness
B) Residence (not traveling)
C) Intention (niyyah)
D) Freedom from menstruation

Key Takeaway
Fasting Ramadan is fard; its validity requires intention and freedom from nullifiers, but not from janabah.

Six Categories of Fasting in the Hanafi School
Classifies fasting into six types with specific rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given fast into one of the six Hanafi categories and state its ruling (fard, wajib, sunnah, mandub, nafl, or makruh).

Core Idea
Fasting is classified into six levels: fard, wajib, sunnah, mandub, nafl, and makruh.

This passage provides a clear taxonomy of fasting according to the Hanafi school. Understanding this hierarchy is essential because each category carries a different legal weight and consequence—for example, only fard and wajib fasts require make-up if broken. The author lists the categories concisely, then gives specific examples for each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six categories from the summary
The text first gives an overview listing six terms: fard, wajib, masnun, mandub, nafl, and makruh. Memorize their order and relative strength.
These are arranged from the most binding (fard) to the least (makruh).
2
Match each category to its specific examples
The passage then provides examples for the first five categories. For fard: Ramadan, expiations, and vows. For wajib: make-up of a broken nafl fast. For sunnah: Ashura with the 9th. For mandub: three white days and Mondays/Thursdays. Nafl and makruh are not detailed here.
Note that the wajib category is uniquely defined as making up a broken nafl fast—this is a Hanafi distinction.
3
Understand the Hanafi distinction between fard and wajib
In Hanafi usul, fard is proven by definitive evidence (qati‘) while wajib is proven by probable evidence (zanni). This classification affects the ruling: fard must be observed without any delay, while wajib allows some flexibility. In this passage, the author signals this distinction by listing them separately.
Other schools often use fard and wajib interchangeably; the Hanafi school is unique in maintaining this as a distinct category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الصوم بالإجمال:
فصل في أقسام الصوم
ينقسم الصوم إلى ستة أقسام:
1- فرض. 2- وواجب. 3- ومسنون. 4- ومندوب. 5- ونفل 6- ومكروه.
أقسام الصوم بالتفصيل:
* أما الفرض فهو:
- صوم رمضان، أداء، وقضاء.
2- وصوم الكفارات والمنذور في الأظهر.
وأما الواجب، فهو: قضاء ما أفسده من نقل.
وأما المسنون، فهو: صوم عاشوراء، مع التاسع.
* وأما المندوب، فهو:
1 صوم ثلاثة من كل شهر، ويُندب كونها الأيام البيض، وهي: الثالث عشر، والرابع عشر والخامس عشر.
2- وصوم يوم الاثنين والخميس.
Summary of the Categories of Fasting: Chapter on the Categories of Fasting. Fasting is divided into six categories: 1. Fard, 2. Wajib, 3. Sunnah, 4. Mandub, 5. Nafl, 6. Makruh. Detailed Categories: As for fard, it is: fasting of Ramadan, performance and make-up; and fasting of expiations and vows according to the most apparent. As for wajib, it is: making up what was spoiled from nafl (supererogatory fasting). As for sunnah, it is: fasting of Ashura with the ninth. As for mandub, it is: fasting three days of every month; and it is recommended that they be the white days, which are the 13th, 14th, and 15th; and fasting Monday and Thursday.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage provides a complete classification of fasting in the Hanafi school. First, it gives an overview: fasting is divided into six legal categories. Then it details each. Fard (compulsory) fasting includes the Ramadan fast (both its performance in time and making up missed days) and, according to the stronger view, fasts of expiation (kaffarah) and vowed fasts (nathr). Wajib (obligatory, but with zanni proof) fasting is only the make-up of a nafl fast that one has broken—this is unique to the Hanafi school. Sunnah fasting is the fast of Ashura (10th of Muharram) together with the 9th. Mandub (recommended) fasting includes fasting three days each month, preferably the three 'white days' (13th, 14th, 15th), and fasting on Mondays and Thursdays. Nafl and makruh are mentioned in the summary but not detailed here; elsewhere in the text they are explained. This distinction is fundamental for differentiating levels of obligation and reward.

Key Terms
فرض (fard): Legally obligatory based on definitive evidence; includes Ramadan fast, expiation, and vows.
واجب (wajib): Obligatory based on probable evidence; here it is specifically the make-up of a spoiled nafl fast.
مسنون (masnun): Sunnah; confirmed recommended practice; example: fasting Ashura with the 9th.
مندوب (mandub): Recommended but not emphasized; includes fasting three white days and Monday/Thursday.
نفل (nafl): Voluntary supererogatory fasting without specific emphasis.
مكروه (makruh): Disliked; not detailed here but refers to fasts that are discouraged.
عاشوراء (‘ashura’): The 10th day of Muharram, a sunnah fast; recommended with the 9th.
الأيام البيض (al-ayyam al-bid): The white days: 13th, 14th, and 15th of each lunar month; recommended for fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of fasting is considered wajib in the Hanafi school?
A) Fasting on Ashura
B) Making up a broken nafl fast
C) Fasting the three white days
D) Fasting on Monday and Thursday

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely classifies fasting into six categories with distinct legal consequences, particularly the unique wajib category of making up a broken nafl fast.

Sawm — Fasting

Fard and Wajib Fast in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining the two obligatory categories of fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of fard and wajib fasting and identify examples of each from the source text.

Core Idea
Hanafi fiqh distinguishes fard (decisive proof) from wajib (probable proof); both are binding but with different epistemological weight.

This passage from Nur al-Idah divides fasting into six categories, beginning with the two obligatory types: fard and wajib. Understanding this distinction is fundamental to Hanafi fiqh, as it affects the basis of obligation and the need for renewal of intention. We will focus on the first two categories and their defining characteristics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six categories
Read the opening sentence to see the overall classification. Note that the passage then details each category. For this lesson, we concentrate on the first two: fard and wajib.
2
Define fard
The text states that fard includes the fast of Ramadan (both its performance and make-up) and, in the most apparent view, fasts of expiation and vows. In Hanafi usul, fard is proven by decisive (qat'i) evidence.
Other schools do not distinguish fard from wajib, but the Hanafi school does.
3
Define wajib
The text specifies that wajib fasting is the make-up of a voluntary fast that one has broken. This is a unique Hanafi ruling: a voluntary act, once begun, becomes binding to complete. Wajib is proven by probable (zanni) evidence.
The category of wajib is narrower than fard; here it applies only to the qada of a spoiled nafl fast.
4
Contrast fard and wajib
Both are binding, but fard is based on stronger evidence. For fasting, fard includes Ramadan, expiation, and vows; wajib includes only broken voluntary fasts. The Hanafi distinction affects the ruling on intention and other details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم
فصل في أقسام الصوم
ينقسم الصوم إلى ستة أقسام: ١- فرض. ٢- وواجب. ٣- ومسنون. ٤- ومندوب. ٥- ونفل. ٦- ومكروه.
أما الفرض فهو: صوم رمضان، أداء، وقضاء. وصوم الكفارات والمنذور في الأظهر. وأما الواجب، فهو: قضاء ما أفسده من نقل. وأما المسنون، فهو: صوم عاشوراء، مع التاسع. وأما المندوب، فهو: ١- صوم ثلاثة من كل شهر، ويُندب كونها الأيام البيض، وهي: الثالث عشر، والرابع عشر والخامس عشر. ٢- وصوم يوم الاثنين والخميس. ٣- وصوم ست من شوال، ثم قيل: الأفضل وصلها، وقيل: تفريقها. وكل صوم ثبت طلبه، والوعد عليه بالسنة، كصوم داود عليه السلام، وهو أفضل الصيام، وأحبه إلى الله تعالى. وأما النفل، فهو: ما سوى ذلك، مما لم تثبت كراهته. وأما المكروه، فهو قسمان: ١- مكروه تنزيهاً. ٢- ومكروه تحريماً. الأول: كصوم عاشوراء مفرداً عن التاسع. والثاني: صوم العيدين، وأيام التشريق.
The Book of Fasting. Chapter on the Divisions of Fasting. Fasting is divided into six divisions: 1. Fard, 2. Wajib, 3. Sunnah, 4. Mandub, 5. Nafl, and 6. Makruh. As for fard, it is: the fast of Ramadan, performance and making up. And the fast of expiations and vows, in the most apparent view. As for wajib, it is: making up what one has spoilt of a voluntary fast. As for sunnah, it is: the fast of Ashura, with the ninth. As for mandub, it is: 1. Fasting three days of every month, and it is recommended that they be the white days, which are the thirteenth, fourteenth and fifteenth. 2. And fasting Monday and Thursday. 3. And fasting six days of Shawwal; then it is said: it is better to connect them, and it is said: to separate them. And every fast for which seeking is established, and a reward is promised in the Sunnah, like the fast of Dawud (peace be upon him), which is the best of fasting and most beloved to Allah Almighty. As for nafl, it is: other than that, for which dislike (karaha) is not established. As for makruh, it is of two types: 1. Makruh tanzihi. 2. And makruh tahrimi. The first: like fasting Ashura alone without the ninth. The second: fasting the two Eids and the days of Tashriq.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that categorizes all types of fasts from a Hanafi perspective. It begins by stating that fasting divides into six categories: fard (obligatory by decisive evidence), wajib (obligatory by probable evidence), sunnah (stressed sunnah), mandub (recommended), nafl (voluntary), and makruh (disliked). The author then defines each category. Fard encompasses the fast of Ramadan (both performance and make-up) and, in the most apparent view, fasts of expiation (kaffara) and vowed fasts (nadhr). Wajib specifically refers to making up a voluntary fast that one has invalidated after having started it—a unique Hanafi ruling that a voluntary act, once engaged, becomes binding to complete. Sunnah includes the fast of Ashura (10th of Muharram) coupled with the 9th. Mandub includes fasting three days each month (preferably the white days: 13th, 14th, 15th), Monday and Thursday, six days of Shawwal (with a difference of opinion on connecting them), and any other fast encouraged by the Sunnah, especially the fast of Dawud (fasting alternate days), which is the most beloved. Nafl covers all other fasts not falling under the previous categories as long as no karaha is established. Makruh is divided into tanzihi (disliked, e.g., fasting Ashura alone without the 9th) and tahrimi (prohibited, e.g., fasting on the two Eids and the days of Tashriq). The passage is a foundational memorization anchor for a student of Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
فرض (fard): An obligation proven by decisive evidence (Quran or mutawatir Sunnah); includes Ramadan and expiations/vows.
واجب (wajib): An obligation proven by probable evidence; in this context, specifically the make-up of a spoilt voluntary fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is classified as wajib fasting?
A) Fasting Ramadan
B) Fasting the day of Ashura with the 9th
C) Making up a voluntary fast that was broken intentionally
D) Fasting on Monday and Thursday

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, fard and wajib are both obligatory but differ in epistemic strength: fard from decisive evidence, wajib from probable evidence, and the only wajib fast is the make-up of a broken voluntary fast.

Sawm — Fasting

Sunnah, Mandub, Nafl, and Makruh Fast in Hanafi Fiqh
Categorizing voluntary and disliked fasts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify non-obligatory fasts into sunnah, mandub, nafl, and the two types of makruh, providing examples from the text.

Core Idea
Non-obligatory fasts in Hanafi fiqh have a detailed hierarchy from stressed sunnah to disliked (makruh), each with its own reward and rulings.

The passage continues with the remaining four categories of fasting: sunnah, mandub, nafl, and makruh (two subcategories). These cover all voluntary and recommended fasts as well as those that are disliked. Mastering this hierarchy helps a Muslim choose the best acts of worship and avoid what is prohibited or disliked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sunnah fasts
The text gives one explicit example: fasting Ashura (10th Muharram) along with the 9th. This is a sunnah mu'akkadah. Note that it must be paired with the 9th to be sunnah; alone it becomes makruh tanzihi.
Ashura fasting is highly recommended; the Prophet (saw) emphasized it.
2
List the mandub fasts
The text lists three white days (13th, 14th, 15th each month), Monday and Thursday, six days of Shawwal, and any fast mentioned in the Sunnah with a reward, like the fast of Dawud (alternate day fasting). These are recommended but not stressed to the level of sunnah.
There is a difference of opinion on whether to connect the six days of Shawwal or separate them; the text mentions both.
3
Define nafl
Nafl is the residual category: any fast for which no dislike (karaha) is established. This includes all voluntary fasts not included in the above categories.
Remember that once you start a nafl fast, it becomes wajib to complete it (covered in the previous lesson).
4
Distinguish the two types of makruh
Makruh tanzihi (lightly disliked) is exemplified by fasting Ashura alone without the 9th. Makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked, nearly haram) includes fasting on the two Eids and the days of Tashriq (11th-13th Dhul Hijjah). The latter is sinful.
Days of Tashriq are after Eid al-Adha; fasting them is forbidden in the Hanafi school.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that categorizes all types of fasts from a Hanafi perspective. It begins by stating that fasting divides into six categories: fard (obligatory by decisive evidence), wajib (obligatory by probable evidence), sunnah (stressed sunnah), mandub (recommended), nafl (voluntary), and makruh (disliked). The passage is a foundational memorization anchor for a student of Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
مسنون (masnun): A stressed sunnah; consistently practiced by the Prophet.
مندوب (mandub): A recommended act, not stressed; includes three white days, Monday/Thursday, six of Shawwal.
نفل (nafl): Voluntary supererogatory fasts with no established disapproval.
مكروه (makruh): Disliked, divided into tanzihi (slightly disliked) and tahrimi (prohibitively disliked, near haram).
أيام التشريق (ayyam al-tashriq): The 11th, 12th, 13th of Dhul Hijjah; fasting on these is makruh tahrimi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a makruh tanzihi fast?
A) Fasting Monday and Thursday
B) Fasting the day of Arafah
C) Fasting Ashura alone without the 9th
D) Fasting the day of Eid al-Adha

Key Takeaway
Non-obligatory fasts range from sunnah (highly recommended) to makruh tahrimi (sinful); following this hierarchy ensures one earns maximum reward and avoids prohibited days.

Recommended Voluntary Fasts: Shawwal, Dawud's Fast, and Nafl
Classification of recommended and voluntary fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the recommended voluntary fasts in the Hanafi school and define nafl as any fast not disliked.

Core Idea
The best voluntary fast is alternate-day fasting as practiced by Prophet Dawud, and six days of Shawwal are recommended.

This lesson covers the recommended voluntary fasts as outlined in the Hanafi text. You will learn about the fast of six days of Shawwal, the superior fast of Dawud, and the general category of nafl fasting that is not disliked. These distinctions help Muslims choose voluntary fasts that carry the most reward while avoiding disliked practices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recommended fasts
Read the passage and list the fasts mentioned as mandub or mustahabb: fasting six days of Shawwal and the fast of Dawud (alternate-day fasting). Note that the text also mentions a difference of opinion on whether to fast the six days consecutively or separately.
The term 'mandub' is used equivalently to mustahabb in this context.
2
Understand the two opinions on Shawwal
The text states 'قيل: الأفضل وصلها، وقيل: تفريقها' – both opinions are valid within the Hanafi school. The preferred view is that joining them is better, but separating is also acceptable.
3
Define nafl fasting
Nafl is defined as any fast whose dislike has not been established. This includes all voluntary fasts that are not makruh (disliked) or haram. Understand that nafl is a broad category that encompasses mandub but also includes permissible acts that are not specifically recommended.
The text says: 'كل صوم ثبت طلبه والوعد عليه بالسنة' – every fast whose seeking is established and promise upon it is by Sunnah – this refers to mandub fasts.
4
Learn about Dawud's fast
The fast of Dawud is described as the most beloved fasting to Allah, consisting of fasting one day and breaking the next. The hadith from Bukhari and Muslim is cited. Recognise this as the optimal voluntary fasting method.
The hadith is quoted in the text: 'أحب الصيام إلى الله تعالى صيام داود...'

Source Text

كتاب الصوم

وصوم ستّ من شوال، ثم قيل : الأفضلُ وَصلُها، وقيل: تفريقها. وكلُّ صوم ثَبَتَ طَلَبُه ، والوعد عليه بالسنة، كصوم داود عليه السلام)، وهو أفضل الصيام، وأحبُّه إلى الله تعالى.
وأما النفل، فهو : ما سوى ذلك، مما لم تثبت كراهته.
وأما المكروه، فهو قسمان:

٢ـ ومكروه تحريماً.
الأول: كصوم عاشوراء مفرداً عن التاسع.
والثاني : صوم العيدين، وأيام التشريق.
و من الصوم المكروه :]

١ - إفراد يوم الجمعة (٢).
٢- وإفراد يوم السبت.
(۱) فقد قال صلى الله عليه وسلم: «أحب الصيام إلى الله تعالى صيام داود، وأحبُّ الصلاة إلى الله صلاة داود كان ينام ،نصفه ويقوم ثلثه، وينام سدسه، وكان يفطر يوماً، ويصوم يوماً». رواه البخاري (١٠٧٩)، ومسلم (١١٥٩).
(۲) قال الطحطاوي . ص ٥٢٨ : وعد في الدر صومه من المندوب، والمعتمد ما هنا. اهـ، ونقل ابن عابدين ١٩٦/٦ عن الفتاوى الخانية ٢٠٦/١ أنه لا بأس بصيامه عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد كما نقل استحبابه عن عامة الحنفية وأن القائلين بالكراهة هم البعض.
ويوم النيروز، والمهرجان) ، إلا أن يُوافق عادته.
وكُره صومُ الوِصال، ولو بين يومين، وهو: أن لا يفطر بعد الغروب أصلاً حتى يتصل صومُ الغدِ بالأمس.
ه - وكُره صوم الدهر (۲
(A)

(۱) هما عيدان من أعياد الفرس. ط.
(۲) وفي الفتاوى الخانية ٢٠٥/١ : إذا أفطر الأيام المنهي عنها العيدين وأيـام التشريق : فلا بأس. وينظر ابن عابدين ١٩٩/٦
Book of Fasting
4- And fasting six days of Shawwal, then it is said: the best is to join them, and it is said: to separate them. And every fast whose seeking is established, and the promise upon it is by the Sunnah, like the fast of Dawud (peace be upon him), and it is the best of fasting, and the most beloved to Allah Almighty.
As for nafl (voluntary), it is: what is other than that, which its dislike has not been established.
As for makruh (disliked), it is two types:
1 - Makruh tanzihan (slightly disliked).
2 - Makruh tahriman (prohibitively disliked).
The first: like fasting Ashura alone without the ninth.
The second: fasting the two Eids, and the days of Tashriq.
And from the disliked fasting:
It is disliked:
1 - To single out Friday for fasting.
2 - To single out Saturday.
(1) He (SAW) said: 'The most beloved fasting to Allah is the fasting of Dawud, and the most beloved prayer to Allah is the prayer of Dawud. He used to sleep half of it, stand a third, and sleep a sixth, and he would break fast one day and fast one day.' Narrated by Bukhari (1079) and Muslim (1159).
(2) Al-Tahtawi said, p. 528: He mentioned in al-Durr that its fast is mandub, but the relied upon is what is here. End quote, and Ibn Abidin (6/196) transmitted from al-Fatawa al-Khaniyya (1/206) that there is no harm in fasting it according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, just as he transmitted its recommendation from the general Hanafis, and that those saying it is disliked are some.
And the day of Nayruz and Mihrajan, unless it coincides with his habit.
And it is disliked to fast wisal (continuous fasting), even between two days, which is: that he does not break his fast after sunset at all until the fast of tomorrow is connected to yesterday.
5 - And it is disliked to fast the whole year (dahr).
(A)
*****
(1) They are two festivals of the Persians.
(2) In al-Fatawa al-Khaniyya (1/205): If he fasts the prohibited days: the two Eids and days of Tashriq, then he has broken [his fast?] and there is nothing. And see Ibn Abidin (6/199).
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah presents the classification of voluntary (nafl) fasting after the obligatory fasts of Ramadan. The author begins by stating that fasting six days of Shawwal is recommended (mandub), with two opinions on whether it is better to fast them consecutively or separately. The text then identifies the best form of voluntary fasting: the fast of Prophet Dawud (AS), which is alternate-day fasting. This is considered the most beloved to Allah.

Nafl is defined as any fast not proven to be disliked. The disliked (makruh) fasts are then divided into two categories: makruh tanzihi (slightly disliked) and makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). Examples of the first type include fasting Ashura alone (without the 9th of Muharram) and singling out Friday or Saturday for fasting. The second type includes fasting on the two Eids and the days of Tashriq (11th-13th of Dhul Hijjah). There is an important intra-Hanafi point regarding Friday: the footnote clarifies that according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, fasting Friday alone is not disliked, and many Hanafis consider it mustahabb. The relied-upon position in the text is that it is makruh, but the later authorities differ.

Other disliked fasts include fasting on the Persian festivals (Nayruz and Mihrajan) unless they coincide with one's habit, continuous fasting (wisal) where one does not break fast after sunset and continues into the next day, and fasting the entire year (dahr) without exception. The footnote on dahr notes that if one misses the prohibited days (Eids and Tashriq), there is no harm, implying the prohibition is specific to those days.

Key Terms
النفل (al-nafl): Voluntary fasting that is not obligatory and not disliked.
المندوب (al-mandub): Recommended act; in this context, fasting six days of Shawwal and Dawud's fast are mandub.
صوم داود (sawm Dawud): Alternate-day fasting; considered the best voluntary fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on fasting six days of Shawwal?
A) It is obligatory (fard).
B) It is recommended (mandub), and joining them is preferred.
C) It is makruh tanzihi.
D) It is permissible but not recommended.

Key Takeaway
The best voluntary fast is alternate-day fasting (Dawud's fast), and fasting six days of Shawwal is recommended but with a preference for consecutiveness.

Sawm — Fasting

Disliked Fasts: Types and Intra-Hanafi Variance on Friday
Classification of disliked fasts with examples and intra-Hanafi variation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between makruh tanzihi and makruh tahrimi fasts and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding fasting on Friday alone.

Core Idea
Fasting on the two Eids and Tashriq days is severely disliked; singling out Friday for fasting is slightly disliked in the relied-upon view, but there is variance.

This lesson focuses on the disliked (makruh) categories of fasting. The Hanafi school divides makruh into two levels: tanzihi (slightly disliked) and tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). You will learn which fasts fall into each category, including the important intra-Hanafi difference on fasting Friday alone. The footnotes provide valuable insight into later fatwa positions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types of makruh
The text explicitly divides makruh into tanzihi and tahrimi. Tanzihi is mildly disliked and avoiding it is better; tahrimi is close to haram and sinful. Identify the characteristics of each.
This distinction is important for prioritizing acts of worship.
2
List examples of makruh tanzihi
The text gives: fasting Ashura alone (without the 9th), singling out Friday for fasting, singling out Saturday, and fasting on Persian festivals (Nayruz and Mihrajan) unless coinciding with habit. Also fasting the whole year (dahr) is included; though the text numbers it as '5' under disliked.
There is a footnote on Friday that introduces ikhtilaf.
3
List examples of makruh tahrimi
Fasting on the two Eids (Fit and Adha) and the days of Tashriq (11-13 Dhul Hijjah) are prohibitively disliked. The text states this clearly.
These are days when fasting is forbidden in the strongest sense short of haram.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi variance on fasting Friday alone
The main text says it is disliked to single out Friday. However, the footnote reports that according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, there is no harm, and many Hanafis consider it mustahabb. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) according to the text is that it is disliked, but later authorities like al-Tahtawi note variance.
This shows the importance of consulting later fatwa works for the operative ruling.

Source Text

وصوم ستّ من شوال، ثم قيل : الأفضلُ وَصلُها، وقيل: تفريقها. وكلُّ صوم ثَبَتَ طَلَبُه ، والوعد عليه بالسنة، كصوم داود عليه السلام)، وهو أفضل الصيام، وأحبُّه إلى الله تعالى.
وأما النفل، فهو : ما سوى ذلك، مما لم تثبت كراهته.
وأما المكروه، فهو قسمان:
۱ - مكروه تنزيهاً.
٢ـ ومكروه تحريماً.
الأول: كصوم عاشوراء مفرداً عن التاسع.
والثاني : صوم العيدين، وأيام التشريق.
و من الصوم المكروه :]
وكُره:
١ - إفراد يوم الجمعة (٢).
٢- وإفراد يوم السبت.
(۱) فقد قال صلى الله عليه وسلم: «أحب الصيام إلى الله تعالى صيام داود، وأحبُّ الصلاة إلى الله صلاة داود كان ينام ،نصفه ويقوم ثلثه، وينام سدسه، وكان يفطر يوماً، ويصوم يوماً». رواه البخاري (١٠٧٩)، ومسلم (١١٥٩).
(۲) قال الطحطاوي . ص ٥٢٨ : وعد في الدر صومه من المندوب، والمعتمد ما هنا. اهـ، ونقل ابن عابدين ١٩٦/٦ عن الفتاوى الخانية ٢٠٦/١ أنه لا بأس بصيامه عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد كما نقل استحبابه عن عامة الحنفية وأن القائلين بالكراهة هم البعض.

وكُره صومُ الوِصال، ولو بين يومين، وهو: أن لا يفطر بعد الغروب أصلاً حتى يتصل صومُ الغدِ بالأمس.

Book of Fasting
4- And fasting six days of Shawwal, then it is said: the best is to join them, and it is said: to separate them. And every fast whose seeking is established, and the promise upon it is by the Sunnah, like the fast of Dawud (peace be upon him), and it is the best of fasting, and the most beloved to Allah Almighty.
As for nafl (voluntary), it is: what is other than that, which its dislike has not been established.
As for makruh (disliked), it is two types:
1 - Makruh tanzihan (slightly disliked).
2 - Makruh tahriman (prohibitively disliked).
The first: like fasting Ashura alone without the ninth.
The second: fasting the two Eids, and the days of Tashriq.
And from the disliked fasting:
It is disliked:
1 - To single out Friday for fasting.
2 - To single out Saturday.
(1) He (SAW) said: 'The most beloved fasting to Allah is the fasting of Dawud, and the most beloved prayer to Allah is the prayer of Dawud. He used to sleep half of it, stand a third, and sleep a sixth, and he would break fast one day and fast one day.' Narrated by Bukhari (1079) and Muslim (1159).
(2) Al-Tahtawi said, p. 528: He mentioned in al-Durr that its fast is mandub, but the relied upon is what is here. End quote, and Ibn Abidin (6/196) transmitted from al-Fatawa al-Khaniyya (1/206) that there is no harm in fasting it according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, just as he transmitted its recommendation from the general Hanafis, and that those saying it is disliked are some.
And the day of Nayruz and Mihrajan, unless it coincides with his habit.
And it is disliked to fast wisal (continuous fasting), even between two days, which is: that he does not break his fast after sunset at all until the fast of tomorrow is connected to yesterday.
5 - And it is disliked to fast the whole year (dahr).
(A)
*****
(1) They are two festivals of the Persians.
(2) In al-Fatawa al-Khaniyya (1/205): If he fasts the prohibited days: the two Eids and days of Tashriq, then he has broken [his fast?] and there is nothing. And see Ibn Abidin (6/199).
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah presents the classification of voluntary (nafl) fasting after the obligatory fasts of Ramadan. The author begins by stating that fasting six days of Shawwal is recommended (mandub), with two opinions on whether it is better to fast them consecutively or separately. The text then identifies the best form of voluntary fasting: the fast of Prophet Dawud (AS), which is alternate-day fasting. This is considered the most beloved to Allah.

Nafl is defined as any fast not proven to be disliked. The disliked (makruh) fasts are then divided into two categories: makruh tanzihi (slightly disliked) and makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). Examples of the first type include fasting Ashura alone (without the 9th of Muharram) and singling out Friday or Saturday for fasting. The second type includes fasting on the two Eids and the days of Tashriq (11th-13th of Dhul Hijjah). There is an important intra-Hanafi point regarding Friday: the footnote clarifies that according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad, fasting Friday alone is not disliked, and many Hanafis consider it mustahabb. The relied-upon position in the text is that it is makruh, but the later authorities differ.

Other disliked fasts include fasting on the Persian festivals (Nayruz and Mihrajan) unless they coincide with one's habit, continuous fasting (wisal) where one does not break fast after sunset and continues into the next day, and fasting the entire year (dahr) without exception. The footnote on dahr notes that if one misses the prohibited days (Eids and Tashriq), there is no harm, implying the prohibition is specific to those days.

Key Terms
مكروه تنزيهاً (makruh tanzihan): Slightly disliked; avoiding it is better but not sinful.
مكروه تحريماً (makruh tahriman): Prohibitively disliked; doing it is sinful.
صوم الوصال (sawm al-wisal): Continuous fasting without breaking at sunset, even for two days.
صوم الدهر (sawm al-dahr): Fasting every day of the year.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in this lesson (considering the footnote), what is the ruling on fasting Friday alone?
A) It is makruh tahrimi.
B) It is makruh tanzihi according to the text, but later authorities permit it.
C) It is absolutely permissible with no dislike.
D) It is haram.

Key Takeaway
Fasting on Eids and Tashriq is severely disliked, while the ruling on fasting Friday alone includes a valid intra-Hanafi variance that leans toward permissibility.

Fasting on Persian Festivals (Nayruz and Mihrajan)
Specifies disliked fasting practices and their exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fasting on Nayruz and Mihrajan and identify when it is not disliked.

Core Idea
Fasting on non-Islamic festivals is disliked unless it coincides with one's regular fast.

The Prophet ﷺ discouraged imitating non-Muslims in their festivals. Therefore, fasting specifically on Nayruz (Persian New Year) and Mihrajan (Persian autumn festival) is makruh tanzih (disliked). However, if a person has a habit of fasting on that day, such as fasting every Monday or Thursday, and it happens to coincide with those days, it is not disliked. This ruling shows that the dislike is due to the intention of celebrating the festival, not the act of fasting itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text states that fasting on Nayruz and Mihrajan is disliked (karaha). Identify the hukm level: makruh tanzih.
2
Note the exception
The phrase 'illa an yuwafiqa 'adatih' (unless it coincides with his habit) indicates an exception. Identify what 'habit' refers to: a regular fast like Mondays/Thursdays.
3
Understand the rationale
The dislike is due to imitating non-Muslims. If one fasts habitually, the intention is not for the festival, so it is not disliked.

Source Text

ويوم النيروز، والمهرجان) ، إلا أن يُوافق عادته.
وكُره صومُ الوِصال، ولو بين يومين، وهو: أن لا يفطر بعد الغروب أصلاً حتى يتصل صومُ الغدِ بالأمس.

And the day of Nayruz and Mihrajan, unless it coincides with his habit. And wisal fasting is disliked, even between two days, which is: not to break the fast after sunset at all until the fast of the next day connects with the previous day. And perpetual fasting is disliked. (1) These are two festivals of the Persians. (2) In al-Fatawa al-Khaniyyah 1/205: If he breaks the forbidden days—the two Eids and the days of Tashriq—then there is no harm. See Ibn Abidin 6/199.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage states rulings on disliked fasts. The author lists several fasts that are makruh tanzih (disliked but not sinful). First, fasting on the Persian festivals Nayruz and Mihrajan is disliked because they are non-Islamic celebrations. However, if a person has a habit of fasting on that day (e.g., Monday/Thursday), it is not disliked. Second, wisal fasting—connecting two days without breaking fast at night—is disliked even if only between two days. This is because it causes excessive hardship and is against the Sunnah (the Prophet forbade it). Third, perpetual fasting (fasting every day of the year including doubtful days) is disliked. The footnote clarifies that if one fasts perpetually but avoids the forbidden days (two Eids and three days of Tashriq), there is no harm. These rulings are from the Hanafi school and are based on hadith and the principle of avoiding hardship.

Key Terms
النيروز (al-Nayruz): Persian New Year, a festival of the Persians.
المهرجان (al-Mihrajan): Persian autumn festival, also considered a non-Islamic celebration.
الوصال (al-wisal): Fasting continuously without breaking fast at sunset for two or more consecutive days.
صوم الدهر (sawm al-dahr): Fasting every day of the year without exception.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Haram and invalid.
B) Makruh tanzih, but not disliked if it coincides with a regular fast.
C) Sunnah because it is a day of celebration.
D) Permissible without any restriction.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on fasting on Persian festivals is makruh tanzih, with exception for habitual fasts.

Wisal Fasting (Continuous Fasting)
Specifies disliked fasting practices and their exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define wisal fasting and state its Hanafi ruling, and explain the reason for its dislike.

Core Idea
Wisal fasting is makruh tanzih even between two days because it causes hardship and contradicts the Sunnah.

Wisal fasting means not breaking the fast at sunset and continuing to fast through the night into the next day. The Prophet ﷺ explicitly forbade this practice out of mercy and to prevent hardship. In the Hanafi school, it is considered makruh tanzih (disliked) even if only between two days. The fast itself is valid, but it is better to avoid it.

Guided Path
1
Define wisal fasting
The text defines it: 'an la yufṭira ba‘da al-ghurūb aṣlan ḥattā yattaṣila ṣawmu al-ghadi bil-amsi.' Identify the key elements: no breaking fast after sunset, connecting two days.
2
State the ruling
The text says 'kurihat ṣawm al-wiṣāl' (wisal fasting is disliked). Identify the hukm level: makruh tanzih.
3
Understand the reason
The reason for dislike is the hardship and the Prophet's prohibition. Even though the fast is valid, it is not recommended.

Perpetual Fasting (Sawm al-Dahr)
Specifies disliked fasting practices and their exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on perpetual fasting and identify the condition that makes it permissible.

Core Idea
Perpetual fasting is disliked in the Hanafi school unless one omits the forbidden days (Eids and Tashriq).

Perpetual fasting (sawm al-dahr) means fasting every day of the year without exception. In the Hanafi school, this is considered makruh tanzih (disliked) because it includes the forbidden days (Eid al-Fitr, Eid al-Adha, and the three days of Tashriq). However, the text clarifies via a footnote from al-Fatawa al-Khaniyyah that if one breaks the fast on those forbidden days, there is no harm. Thus, perpetual fasting that avoids the prohibited days is not disliked and can be meritorious.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text states 'kurihat ṣawm al-dahr' (perpetual fasting is disliked).
2
Locate the exception
The footnote from al-Fatawa al-Khaniyyah says: if one breaks the fast on the forbidden days (Eids and Tashriq), then there is no harm. This is the key exception.
3
Understand the rationale
The dislike is due to the inclusion of forbidden days. Avoiding those days removes the dislike.

Intention Requirements for Fasting: When Night Intention and Specification Are Not Required
Explains fasts that do not require night intention or specifying

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which fasts do not require night intention or specifying, and apply the intention rules for healthy resident, traveler, and sick person.

Core Idea
Ramadan, specified vows, and voluntary fasts do not require night intention or specifying; a general intention suffices.

This passage clarifies a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh regarding the intention for fasting. While many scholars require the intention to be made at night and to specify the type of fast for all obligatory fasts, the Hanafi school relaxes this for certain categories. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for correctly fulfilling one's fasts, especially when one forgets or is unable to make the intention at night.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
List the fasts that do not require night intention (tabayyut) nor specifying (ta'yin): Ramadan, a vow with a specified time, and voluntary (nafl) fasts.
These are exceptions to the general rule that intention must be made at night and specified.
2
Understand the time window for intention
For these fasts, intention is valid from the night until just before midday (the time of dhawah kubra, roughly halfway between dawn and noon).
This means you can decide to fast after waking up, as long as it's before that time.
3
Distinguish rulings for different persons
A healthy resident who intends another obligatory fast (e.g., a missed day) or even a voluntary fast will have it count as Ramadan. A traveler, however, will have it count as the intended wajib, not Ramadan.
Traveler's exception: his intention is effective for what he intended.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on the sick person
For a sick person, there are two views: (a) it counts as what he intended (the view of al-Hidayah and most Bukharan scholars), (b) it counts for Ramadan (authenticated by a group of Hanafis).
The text does not give a final tarjih between these views.
5
Apply the rule for specified vows
A specified vow (e.g., fasting the first of Muharram) must be intended specifically; intending another wajib does not make it valid for the vow.
This contrasts with Ramadan, where a general intention can suffice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل
في ما لا يُشترط تبييت النية، وتعيينها فيه
ما لا يشترط فيه تعيين النية : ]
وما يُشترط
أما القسم الذي لا يُشترط فيه تعيين النية، ولا تبييتها، فهو: أ - أداء رمضان. ب ـ والنذرُ المُعيَّنُ زمانه.
ج - والنفلُ.
١ - فيصح بنية من الليل إلى ما قبل نصف النهار، على الأصح.
ونصف النهار : من طلوع الفجر إلى وقت الضحوة الكبرى. ٢ - ويصح أيضاً بمُطلَق النية، وبنية النفل، ولو كان مسافراً، أو مريضاً، في الأصح. ويصح أداء رمضانَ بنية واجب آخر، لمن كان صحيحاً، مقيماً.

(۱) فقيل : يقع عما نوى، وبهذا قال صاحب الهداية وأكثـر مشايخ بخارى، وقيل : يقع عن رمضان، وصححه فريق من الحنفية. مراقي.
Chapter on what does not require night intention and specifying the intention. What does not require specifying the intention: And what requires. As for the category that does not require specifying the intention nor night intention, it is: a) Observing Ramadan. b) A vow whose time is specified. c) Voluntary fasts. 1. It is valid by intention from night until before midday, according to the most correct view. And midday: from dawn to the time of the great forenoon. 2. It is also valid with absolute intention, and with intention of voluntary fast, even if one is a traveler or sick, according to the most correct view. And observing Ramadan is valid with intention of another obligatory act, for one who is healthy and resident. Contrary to the traveler: then it counts as what he intended of the obligatory act. And the preponderance differed concerning the sick person if he intended another obligatory act in Ramadan. (1) It was said: it counts as what he intended, and this is what the author of al-Hidayah and most scholars of Bukhara said. And it was said: it counts for Ramadan, and a group of Hanafis authenticated it. (Maraqi). 6. And a vow whose time is specified is not valid by intention of another obligatory act, but rather it counts as what he intended.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage discusses the intention conditions for various types of fasts. It divides fasts into those that require specifying the intention (ta'yin al-niyyah) and those that do not, and those that require the intention to be made at night (tabayyut al-niyyah) and those that do not. The category that does not require either specifying or night intention includes: (a) Ramadan fast, (b) a vow with a specified time (e.g., 'I will fast the first of Muharram'), and (c) voluntary (nafl) fasts. For these, the intention can be made up until just before noon (dhahwah kubra, which is the midpoint between dawn and noon). The most correct position is that even an absolute intention (e.g., intending to fast without specifying it as Ramadan) suffices for Ramadan, as does intending a voluntary fast (even if one is a traveler or sick). For a healthy resident, if he intends another obligatory fast (like making up a missed day), it still counts for Ramadan. However, a traveler’s intention for another wajib fast counts for that other wajib, not for Ramadan. For a sick person, there is an ikhtilaf: some say it counts as what he intended, others say it counts for Ramadan. The latter is authenticated by some Hanafis. For a specified vow, it must be intended specifically; intending another wajib does not count.

Key Terms
تبييت النية (tabayyut al-niyyah): Making the intention for fasting during the night (from Maghrib to dawn).
تعيين النية (ta'yin al-niyyah): Specifying which type of fast one intends (e.g., Ramadan, Qada, Nadhr).
الضحوة الكبرى (al-dhahwah al-kubra): The time roughly halfway between dawn and noon; the latest time for making intention for these fasts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a healthy resident intends another wajib fast (e.g., a missed day) on a Ramadan day before midday, what is the ruling?
A) The fast is invalid because intention must be specifically for Ramadan.
B) The fast counts as the intended wajib (missed day).
C) The fast counts as the Ramadan fast.
D) The fast is valid only if he also intends voluntary.

Key Takeaway
For Ramadan, specified vows, and voluntary fasts, intention can be made up to midday, and a general intention suffices, with special rulings for travelers and a dispute on the sick.

Fasts That Do Not Require Specified or Night Intention
Lists fasts not requiring ta'yin or tabyit with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which fasts do not require specifying the intention (ta'yin) nor making it at night (tabyit) and explain the valid time window for intention.

Core Idea
For Ramadan, time-specific vows, and nafl fasts, a general intention before midday suffices; specification is not required.

This lesson breaks down the first category of fasts according to the Hanafi school: those that do not require you to specify which fast you are performing (ta'yin) nor to make the intention at night (tabyit). You will learn the three types, the time window for intention, and special rulings for travelers, sick persons, and those intending other obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
List the three fasts: Ramadan (adā'), a vowed fast with a predetermined time (nadhr mu'ayyan zamānuhu), and supererogatory fast (nafl).
These are the only fasts in this category.
2
Understand the time window
The intention for these fasts is valid from night (after Maghrib) up to just before midday. Midday here means from dawn (fajr) to al-dahwa al-kubra (roughly when the sun is fully risen, before zuhr).
This is a leniency not given to other obligatory fasts.
3
Note the sufficiency of absolute intention
An absolute intention (simply intending to fast, without specifying which fast) is valid for these categories, including for a traveler or sick person intending nafl.
Absolute intention means just 'I intend to fast'.
4
Examine special cases
For a healthy resident who intends another obligatory fast (e.g., a vow) during Ramadan, it counts as Ramadan. For a traveler, it counts as the intended obligation. For a sick person, there is a difference of opinion (relied view: it counts as intended obligation).
Refer to the marginal note in the text.
5
Distinguish the time-specific vow
Unlike the above, a time-specific vow cannot be replaced by intending another obligation; if you intend another fast on that day, the intention does not fulfill the vowed fast.
The vowed fast remains a debt.

Book of Fasting
Section
On what does not require making the intention at night, nor specifying it, and what requires it
What does not require specifying the intention:
As for the category which does not require specifying the intention nor making it at night, it is: (a) Performing the Ramadan fast, (b) A vowed fast with a specified time, (c) A supererogatory fast.
1. It is valid with an intention from the night up to before midday, according to the most sound opinion. And midday is from dawn to the time of the great forenoon (al-dahwa al-kubra). 2. It is also valid with an absolute intention, and with the intention of a supererogatory fast, even if the person is traveling or sick, according to the most sound opinion. And performing the Ramadan fast with the intention of another obligation is valid for one who is healthy and resident. As opposed to the traveler: for it falls under what he intended of the obligation. And the weighting differs concerning the sick person if he intended another obligation during Ramadan (1). 6. And a vowed fast with a specified time is not valid with the intention of another obligation; rather it falls under what he intended of the obligation.
(1) It is said: it falls under what he intended, and this is the view of the author of al-Hidayah and most scholars of Bukhara, and it is said: it falls under Ramadan, and this was corrected by a group of Hanafis. Maraqi.
What requires specifying the intention:
As for the second category, which requires specifying the intention and [making it] at night (2), it is: 1. Making up Ramadan fasts. 2. Making up what he spoiled of a supererogatory fast. 3. The fasts of expiations of various kinds. 4. An absolute vowed fast, such as one saying: If Allah cures my sick relative, I must fast a day, and the cure occurs.
*****
(1) i.e., what the vower intended of an obligation other than the vowed one, in all narrations, and the vowed one remains a debt on him, and he makes it up. Maraqi with al-Tahawi.
(2) i.e., at night.
Page 532
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah discusses the intention requirements for fasting. It divides fasts into two categories: those that do not require specifying which fast one is performing (ta'yin) nor making the intention at night (tabyit), and those that require both. 

The first category includes: (a) the obligatory Ramadan fast, (b) a vowed fast whose time is predetermined (e.g., 'I will fast on Friday'), and (c) any supererogatory (nafl) fast. For these, the intention may be made from the beginning of the night up to just before midday. Midday here is defined as from dawn (fajr) until the 'great forenoon' (al-dahwa al-kubra), which is just before zuhr. Furthermore, an absolute intention (simply intending to fast) is sufficient; it does not need to be specified as Ramadan or nafl. Even a traveler or sick person may validly intend a supererogatory fast during Ramadan without specifying. A healthy resident may intend another obligation (like a vowed fast) during Ramadan and it counts as Ramadan, not the other obligation. However, a traveler intending another obligation during Ramadan counts for that obligation, not Ramadan. For a sick person, there is a difference of opinion: according to the author of al-Hidayah and most Bukhara scholars, it counts as the intended obligation, but some Hanafis hold it counts as Ramadan. The more relied-upon view may be the first. Importantly, a vowed fast with a specified time cannot be replaced by intending another obligation; it remains due.

The second category requires both specifying the intent (e.g., 'I intend to make up a missed Ramadan fast') and making the intention at night. This includes: making up missed Ramadan days, making up a spoiled supererogatory fast (since the original was valid and needs qada'), expiatory fasts (kaffarah) of various kinds, and an absolute vowed fast (e.g., 'If Allah cures my relative, I must fast a day') where the time is not fixed. The key distinction is that these fasts have specific obligations that must be clearly intended, and the intention must be made before dawn.

Key Terms
تبييت النية (tabyit al-niyya): Making the intention at night, i.e., before the break of dawn.
تعيين النية (ta'yin al-niyya): Specifying which particular fast one intends to perform (e.g., Ramadan or a specific vow).
نذر معين زمانه (nadhr mu'ayyan zamanuhu): A vow in which the time of fasting is predetermined, such as vowing to fast a specific day.
الضحوة الكبرى (al-dahwa al-kubra): The great forenoon, roughly the time when the sun has fully risen and just before the zenith, marking the end of the valid intention window for these fasts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a healthy resident intends another obligatory fast (e.g., a vow) while performing the Ramadan fast?
A) It counts as Ramadan.
B) It counts as the intended obligation.
C) The fast is invalid.
D) It counts as nafl.

Key Takeaway
Ramadan, time-specific vows, and nafl fasts accept a general intention made before midday, without specification or night intention.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
10 min
Fasts That Require Specified and Night Intention
Lists fasts that require ta'yin and tabyit with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of fasts that require both specifying the intention (ta'yin) and making it at night (tabyit), and apply the rule to new cases.

Core Idea
Making up missed fasts, expiatory fasts, and absolute vowed fasts demand a specific intention made before dawn.

The second category of fasts according to the Hanafi school includes those that cannot be performed with a general intention or a late intention. This lesson covers the four types: qada of Ramadan, qada of a spoiled nafl, expiatory fasts (kaffarah), and absolute vowed fasts. You will learn why they require both specification and night intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types
List the four fasts that require both ta'yin and tabyit: making up missed Ramadan days (qada Ramadan), making up a spoiled supererogatory fast (qada nafl), expiatory fasts (kaffarah), and an absolute vowed fast (nadhr mutlaq).
These are debts that must be clearly intended.
2
Understand why specification is required
Because these fasts are specific obligations with distinct identities (e.g., qada vs. kaffarah). Without specification, one cannot fulfill the correct debt.
In contrast, Ramadan is a fixed time obligation that overrides others.
3
Understand why night intention is required
Because these are obligatory fasts that require full commitment from the start of the day. The intention must be made before dawn (tabyit).
This is stricter than the first category.
4
Define an absolute vowed fast
An absolute vowed fast is one where the time is not specified, e.g., 'I vow to fast a day if Allah cures my relative.' The fulfillment day is chosen by the person; hence it requires specification.
Distinguish from time-specific vow which is in the first category.
5
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given a new case, determine if it falls in this category and thus requires ta'yin and tabyit. Example: 'I must fast three days for breaking an oath.' That is kaffarah, so it requires both.
Always check the type of fast first.

Key Terms
قضاء رمضان (qada Ramadan): Making up missed days of Ramadan fasting.
صوم الكفارات (sawm al-kaffarat): Expiatory fasts required as a penalty for certain sins or violations, e.g., breaking an oath, zihar, intentional breaking of Ramadan.
نذر مطلق (nadhr mutlaq): An absolute vow in which the time of fasting is not predetermined, e.g., vowing to fast a day if a condition is met.
قضاء ما أفسده من نفل (qada ma afsadahu min nafl): Making up a supererogatory fast that one invalidated after starting it.

Key Takeaway
Qada Ramadan, qada of spoiled nafl, expiatory fasts, and absolute vowed fasts require both a specific intention and a night-time intention.

Fasts Requiring Specified Night Intention
Listing four fasts requiring tabyit and ta'yin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four categories of fasts that require both tabyit and ta'yin of intention, and explain the rule for absolute vowed fasts.

Core Idea
Four obligatory fasts must be intended by night and specified: qada of Ramadan, broken voluntary, expiations, and absolute vows.

Intention (niyyah) is central to the validity of worship. For obligatory fasts, the Hanafi school requires the intention to be made at night (tabyit) and to specify which fast is being observed (ta'yin). This passage from Nur al-Idah lists the four categories of fasts that demand both conditions. Understanding these categories is essential for correctly fulfilling one's obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
The passage begins by stating the second category of actions requiring specification of intention (ta'yin) and night intention (tabyit). Recognize that the first category (not mentioned here) deals with actions where intention is not required at night.
The term 'tabyit' means making the intention during the night before dawn.
2
List the Four Categories
The text enumerates four items: (1) qada of Ramadan, (2) qada of a broken voluntary fast, (3) expiation fasts of all types, and (4) absolute vowed fasts. Memorize this list.
Each of these fasts is obligatory (wajib) and thus requires deliberate intention.
3
Analyze the Absolute Vow Example
The passage gives a concrete example of an absolute vow: 'If Allah cures my sick person, then I must fast a day.' This is a conditional vow (nadhr) that becomes binding upon fulfillment of the condition.
The footnote clarifies that the vowed fast remains on the person's liability until performed, and it is distinct from other obligatory fasts.
4
Understand the Footnote Clarifications
The footnotes explain that the vowed fast is a separate obligation from any other obligatory fast (e.g., qada), and 'tabyit' means at night. This reinforces the need to specify the intended fast in the intention.
The reference to Maraqi and al-Tahtawi indicates later Hanafi commentaries.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement specifying which categories of obligatory fasts require both specification of intention (ta'yin al-niyyah) and night intention (tabyit al-niyyah). The text lists four types: qada of Ramadan, qada of a broken voluntary fast, expiation fasts (kaffarat), and absolute vowed fasts (mandhur mutlaq). An absolute vow is exemplified by a conditional promise: 'If Allah cures my sick, then I must fast a day.' The footnote clarifies that if someone makes such a vow and then fulfills it by fasting, the vowed fast remains on his liability until performed, and it is distinct from any other obligatory fasts he may have. The term 'tabyit' is explained as meaning 'at night.'

Key Terms
تبييتها (tabyituha): making the intention at night (i.e., before dawn)
المنذور المطلق (al-mandhur al-mutlaq): an absolute vow, such as a conditional vow without specifying a particular type of fast
