Confirmation of Ramadan and the Day of Doubt
States how Ramadan is confirmed and rules for fasting on the day of doubt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two methods of confirming Ramadan, define the day of doubt, and apply the Hanafi rulings on fasting on that day for both the common person and the elite.

Core Idea
Ramadan is confirmed by sighting or completing Sha'ban; the day of doubt is makruh except for a firmly intended voluntary fast.

This lesson covers the fundamental Hanafi rulings on how Ramadan is established and the complex rules surrounding the day of doubt. You will learn why intention is critical on this ambiguous day and how the rulings differ between the general public and those with deeper religious commitment. The text from Nur al-Idah provides a clear, memorizable summary that distinguishes valid and invalid fasting on this day.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods of confirming Ramadan
The passage states that Ramadan is established either by sighting the crescent (ru'ya) or by completing Sha'ban as thirty days if the crescent is obscured. Note that the second method is based on the principle that the lunar month cannot exceed 30 days.
Both methods are equally valid and derive from the Prophet's hadith: 'Fast when you see it and break when you see it.'
2
Define the day of doubt (yawm al-shakk)
The day of doubt is the day after the 29th of Sha'ban when the moon is obscured, so one cannot know whether it is the 30th of Sha'ban or the 1st of Ramadan. The 'two sides of knowledge and ignorance are equal' means there is no preponderant indication.
The day of doubt only occurs if the moon is obscured on the 29th; if it is clear, the ruling is different.
3
Understand the general ruling on fasting the day of doubt
All fasting on this day is disliked (makruh) except for a voluntary fast that one firmly intends without any hesitation. The hesitation invalidates the intention and thus the fast. Even if the day later turns out to be Ramadan, a valid voluntary fast suffices for Ramadan.
The dislike is tanzihi (slight), not tahrimi (prohibited).
4
Distinguish between the common person and the elite
The mufti orders the common people to wait without intending to fast; if the time of intention (forenoon) passes without clarity, they must break their fast. The elite (mufti, judge, and those who can control their intention) are to fast because they can maintain a firm voluntary intention without any thought of it being the obligatory Ramadan fast.
The elite's ability to avoid hesitation makes their fast valid and even recommended to preserve the sanctity of the day.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم
[ما يثبت به رمضان : ] يثبت رمضان:
فصل
فيما يَثْبُتُ به الهلالُ
وفي صوم يوم الشك، وغيره
١ - برؤية هلاله. ٢ - أو بِعَد شعبان ثلاثينَ إِن غُمَّ الهلالُ.
[يوم الشك : ]
١ - ويوم الشك هو : ما يلي التاسع والعشرين من شعبان، وقد استوى فيه طَرَفُ العلم والجهل، بأن غُمَّ الهلال.
آخر.
٢- وكره فيه كل صوم ، إلا صومَ نفل جَزَمَ به بلا ترديد بينه وبين صوم
٣- وإن ظهر أنه من رمضان: أجزأ عنه ما صامه.
٤- وإن ردد فيه بين صيام وفطر: لا يكون صائماً. ٥- وكره صوم يوم أو يومين من آخر شعبان، لا ما فوقهما.
ويأمر المفتي العامةَ بالتلوم (۱) يومَ الشك، ثم بالإفطار إذا ذهب
وقت النية)، ولم يتبيَّن الحال.
(۱) أي بالانتظار بلا نية صوم في ابتداء . يوم الشك. مراقي.
(۲) وهو وقت الضحوة الكبرى.
(۳) أي نفلاً.
(٤) أي في القضاء.
ويصوم فيه المفتي، والقاضي، ومَن كان من الخواص، وهو: مَن يتمكن من ضبط نفسه عن الترديد في النية، وملاحظة كونه عن الفرض.
Book of Fasting. [What Ramadan is established by:] Ramadan is established: Chapter on what the crescent is established by and on fasting the day of doubt and other than it. 1- By seeing its crescent. 2- Or by completing Sha'ban as thirty if the crescent is obscured. [The day of doubt:] 1- The day of doubt is: what follows the twenty-ninth of Sha'ban, and the two sides of knowledge and ignorance are equal in it, because the crescent is obscured. End. 2- All fasting is disliked in it, except for a voluntary fast firmly intended without hesitation between it and fasting. 3- And if it becomes clear that it is from Ramadan, what he fasted suffices for it. 4- And if he hesitated between fasting and breaking, he is not a fasting person. 5- It is disliked to fast a day or two days from the end of Sha'ban, not more than that. The mufti orders the common people to wait on the day of doubt, then to break the fast if the time of intention passes and the situation has not become clear. (1) i.e., waiting without intention of fasting at the beginning of the day of doubt. (2) It is the time of forenoon. (3) i.e., as a voluntary act. (4) i.e., in making up. And the mufti, the judge, and those of the elite fast on it, and he is: the one who can control himself from hesitation in the intention and from considering it as obligatory.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah, a foundational Hanafi matn, presents the rulings on confirming the start of Ramadan and the rules concerning the day of doubt. The author first states that Ramadan is established either by sighting the crescent moon or, if the moon is obscured, by completing the month of Sha'ban to thirty days. This is the standard Hanafi method for determining the start of the month. The focus then shifts to the day of doubt, which is the day after the twenty-ninth of Sha'ban when the moon is obscured, making it uncertain whether the next day is the thirtieth of Sha'ban or the first of Ramadan. The ruling is that fasting on this day is makruh (disliked) in general. However, an exception is made for a voluntary fast that one has firmly intended without any hesitation between it being a voluntary fast or a Ramadan fast. If it later becomes clear that it was Ramadan, that fast will suffice for the obligatory Ramadan fast. If a person hesitates in his intention between fasting and breaking, his fast is not valid. It is also specifically disliked to fast one or two days before Ramadan (i.e., the last day or two of Sha'ban), but fasting three or more days is not disliked. The text then addresses the role of the mufti (the jurist-consultant) on the day of doubt: he instructs the general public to wait without intending to fast, and if the time of intention (the forenoon of that day) passes without clarity, they should break their fast. As for the mufti, the judge, and other elite scholars, they are to fast on the day of doubt because they are capable of maintaining a proper intention without hesitation and without intending it as the obligatory Ramadan fast. This distinction between the common people and the elite highlights the Hanafi principle that intention (niyyah) is crucial in acts of worship.

Key Terms
هلال (hilāl): The crescent moon at the beginning of the lunar month, specifically for Ramadan and Shawwal.
يوم الشك (yawm al-shakk): The day after the 29th of Sha'ban when the moon is obscured, making it uncertain whether it is the 30th of Sha'ban or the 1st of Ramadan.
نية (niyyah): Intention, a prerequisite for valid fasting. The text distinguishes between a firm intention (without hesitation) and a hesitant one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for a common person on the day of doubt if the time of intention passes without clarity?
A) He must continue fasting in case it is Ramadan.
B) He must break his fast.
C) He can choose to fast or break.
D) His fast is automatically valid.

Key Takeaway
The day of doubt requires careful intention: the general public should wait and break, while the elite may fast with a firm voluntary intention.

Ruling for a Person Who Sees the Crescent Alone
States the ruling for a person who alone sees the crescent of Ramadan or Shawwal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a person who alone sees the crescent of Ramadan or Shawwal and explain the consequences of breaking the fast.

Core Idea
Personal certainty does not override judicial rejection; the individual must still fast and make up if he breaks.

This concise passage from Nur al-Idah addresses a specific scenario: a single individual sights the crescent moon of either Ramadan or Shawwal, but his testimony is rejected by the judge. The Hanafi school imposes a personal obligation on that individual to fast despite the rejection, and it clarifies the consequences if he breaks his fast. Understanding this ruling clarifies the relationship between personal knowledge and public judicial authority in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage covers two distinct cases: (1) a person who alone sees the crescent of Ramadan, and (2) a person who alone sees the crescent of Shawwal. In both cases, his testimony is rejected. Note the different rulings for each.
The ruling depends on which crescent is sighted.
2
Apply the ruling to each scenario
For Ramadan: fasting is incumbent (lazim) upon him. For Shawwal: it is not permissible for him to break his fast. In either case, if he breaks the fast, he must make it up (qada) but no expiation (kaffarah) is required.
The phrase 'in the two times' refers to breaking at the end of Ramadan or at the start of Shawwal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رؤية الواحد للهلال :]
١- ومن رأى هلال رمضانَ أو الفطر وحده، ورُدَّ قوله : لَزِمَه
الصيام.
٢- ولا يجوز له الفطر بتيقنه هلال شوال.
وإن أفطر في الوقتين : قضى ، ولا كفارة عليه ولو كان فطره قبل ما
رده القاضي، في الصحيح.
Sighting of the crescent by one person:] 1- And whoever sees the crescent of Ramadan or that of the breaking of the fast alone, and his statement is rejected, fasting is incumbent upon him. 2- And it is not permissible for him to break the fast with his certainty of the crescent of Shawwal. And if he breaks the fast in the two times (i.e., if he breaks at the end of Ramadan or at the beginning of Shawwal), he makes up (the fast) and there is no expiation upon him, even if his breaking was before the judge rejected his testimony, according to the sound opinion.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This short paragraph addresses the situation of a single person who sees the crescent moon of Ramadan or Shawwal but whose testimony is rejected (e.g., because he is not deemed a trustworthy witness by the judge). For Ramadan, even if his sighting is not accepted in court, he must personally fast. For Shawwal, he is not allowed to break his fast even if he is certain that he saw the crescent of Shawwal. If he does break his fast in either case—i.e., by not fasting on what he believes is Ramadan or by breaking on what he believes to be the day of Eid—he must make up that day (qada) but there is no expiation (kaffarah) upon him, even if he broke before the judge officially rejected his testimony. The phrase 'in the two times' refers to the two scenarios: when he thinks it is Ramadan or when he thinks it is Shawwal. The ruling is from the sound (sahih) view of the Hanafi school. The key principle is that personal certainty does not permit one to act against the public ruling based on judicial rejection; however, since the person acted on his own belief, the fast is not valid and must be made up, but no kaffarah due to the ambiguity.

Key Terms
رؤية الواحد (ru'yat al-wāhid): Sighting of the crescent moon by a single individual, not two witnesses.
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation for deliberately breaking a Ramadan fast without excuse; the text says no kaffarah applies in this specific case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person sees the crescent of Shawwal alone and is certain it is Eid, but the judge rejects his testimony, what is his obligation?
A) He may break his fast since he is certain.
B) He must still fast and cannot break.
C) He must break but make up later.
D) He may break and no qada is required.

Key Takeaway
A person who alone sees the crescent of Ramadan must fast, and one who alone sees Shawwal must not break, even if his testimony is rejected; breaking requires qada without kaffarah.

Acceptance of One Witness for Ramadan with Cloudy Sky
States that one just witness is accepted for Ramadan when the sky is cloudy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which a single just witness suffices for establishing Ramadan in the Hanafi school and distinguish between 'adl and mastur witnesses.

Core Idea
When the sky is obscured, one just witness suffices for Ramadan; in clear skies, two are required.

This lesson presents a specific concession in the Hanafi school: when the sky is cloudy, dusty, or similarly obscured, the testimony of a single just witness ('adl) is accepted to confirm the start of Ramadan. Normally, Islamic law requires two upright witnesses for moon sighting, but this ruling eases the requirement due to difficulty. We will also clarify who qualifies as 'adl versus mastur (unknown integrity), as only the former's testimony is accepted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Find the key phrase 'إذا كان بالسماء علة' (when there is an obstruction in the sky). This triggers the relaxation.
Obstructions include clouds, dust, and similar.
2
State the ruling
Under that condition, the report of one 'adl (just) person is accepted. This means we act on it and begin fasting.
3
Define 'adl and mastur
The footnote defines 'adl as one whose good deeds outweigh bad; mastur is one whose character is unknown—neither proven sinful nor just. Only 'adl is accepted.
Other schools may define 'adl differently.
4
Contrast with clear sky
Implied ruling: when sky is clear, two 'adl witnesses are required. This is the default.
This is not in the text but is a necessary inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثبوت هلال رمضان إذا كان بالسماء علة :]
وإذا كان بالسماء علة : من غَيْم، أو غبار، ونحوه:
۱ - قبل خبر واحد عدل(٥).
(٥) العدل هو من حسناته أكثر من سيئاته، وأما المستور فهو : مجهول الحال،
لم يظهر له فسق ولا عدالة. مراقي.
Establishment of the crescent of Ramadan when there is an obstruction in the sky:] And when there is an obstruction in the sky: from clouds, or dust, and the like: 1- The report of one just (person) is accepted. (5) The just (al-'adl) is one whose good deeds are more than his evil deeds. As for the mastur (concealed), he is one of unknown condition, no sin nor justice has appeared from him. Maraqi.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage gives the Hanafi ruling for accepting testimony when there is an atmospheric obstruction such as clouds, dust, or similar. In such a situation, the report of a single just ( 'adl ) witness is accepted to establish the beginning of Ramadan. The footnote clarifies that an 'adl person is one who is known to have more good deeds than bad deeds, i.e., one who is outwardly upright. In contrast, a mastur (concealed) person is one whose state is unknown—neither known to be sinful nor just—and such a person's testimony is not accepted. This leniency in accepting one witness for Ramadan in obstructed skies is a specific concession of the Hanafi school, as normally two witnesses are required for sighting the moon. The rationale is that when the moon is obscured, it is more difficult to sight, so the condition is relaxed.

Key Terms
عدل (ʿadl): A witness who is just, meaning his good deeds outweigh his bad deeds; his testimony is accepted.
مستور (mastūr): A person of unknown condition regarding justice; his testimony is not accepted.
علة (ʿillah): Here means an obstruction in the sky such as clouds or dust that prevents sighting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) If the witness is a close relative of the judge.
B) If the sky is clear.
C) If there is an obstruction in the sky like clouds or dust.
D) If the witness is a mastur (concealed) person.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts a single 'adl witness for Ramadan only when the sky is obstructed; otherwise, two witnesses are required.

Day of Doubt: Public Waiting vs Elite Fasting
Ruling on day of doubt: public vs elite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the general public and the elite on the day of doubt, and explain the rationale for the distinction.

Core Idea
On the day of doubt, the public waits and breaks; the elite fasts secretly as nafl.

The day of doubt (30th Sha'ban) presents a unique legal situation: the public cannot risk fasting, while the elite, due to their knowledge and self-control, can secretly fast as a voluntary act. This lesson unpacks the precise ruling and the reasoning behind the public–elite distinction based on the Hanafi textual tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
The passage distinguishes between the 'general public' (‘āmmah) and the 'elite' (khawāṣṣ). Note that the elite includes the mufti, the qadi, and anyone who can control their intention without doubting that the fast might be obligatory.
The elite are not defined by social status but by legal capacity to manage intention.
2
State the ruling for the general public
The mufti orders the public to 'wait' (talawwum) on the day of doubt without making a firm intention to fast. If the time for intention (al-ḍaḥwah al-kubrā) passes and the situation remains unclear, they must break their fast (i.e., not continue as a Ramaḍān fast).
The waiting period is until the end of the time for making the intention (roughly mid-morning).
3
State the ruling for the elite
The elite (mufti, qadi, and those who can control their intention) are permitted to fast secretly with the intention of a voluntary (nafl) fast. If it later becomes Ramaḍān, it counts; if not, it remains nafl.
They must not publicise their fast to avoid misunderstanding.
4
Explain the wisdom of the distinction
The public might wrongly enter an obligatory fast with doubt, which is not permitted. The elite, due to their knowledge and precise intention, can perform a valid nafl fast that is automatically upgraded if the day turns out to be Ramaḍān.
This is a unique Hanafi perspective; other schools may rule differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦ - ويأمر المفتي العامةَ بالتلوم (1) يومَ الشك، ثم بالإفطار إذا ذهب وقت النية)، ولم يتبيَّن الحال. ويصوم فيه المفتي، والقاضي، ومَن كان من الخواص، وهو: مَن يتمكن من ضبط نفسه عن الترديد في النية، وملاحظة كونه عن الفرض.
6. The mufti orders the general public to wait on the day of doubt, then to break the fast if the time of intention has passed and the situation has not become clear. The mufti, the qadi, and those who are from the elite fast on it, and they are those who are able to control themselves from hesitation in the intention and observing that it is for the obligatory [fast].
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This is a ruling on the day of doubt (yawm al-shakk), which is the 30th of Sha'ban when the moon is not sighted. The general public are instructed to wait without intending an obligatory fast, and if the moon is not confirmed by the time of الضحوة الكبرى (late morning), they break their fast (i.e., they do not continue as a Ramadan fast). The elite (mufti, qadi, and those who can control their intention) secretly fast with the intention of a voluntary fast, but if it later turns out to be Ramadan, it counts as Ramadan; if not, it is nafl. The key is that the elite do not publicly fast to avoid confusion or false reports.

Key Terms
التلوم (al-talawwum): Waiting/lingering without committing to a fast from the beginning of the day.
الخواص (al-khawāṣṣ): The elite: those who can control themselves from doubting the intention and can intend a non-obligatory fast while secretly observing the day of doubt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Fast with the intention of Ramadan
B) Wait without fasting until the time for intention passes, then break if unclear
C) Fast a voluntary fast secretly
D) Celebrate as if it is Eid

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between the general public (who must wait and break) and the elite (who may fast secretly as nafl) on the day of doubt, based on the ability to control intention.

6 min
Establishing Ramadan Crescent When Sky is Cloudy
Details acceptance of one witness's testimony for Ramadan when cloudy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for accepting testimony to establish the Ramadan crescent when the sky is cloudy and identify which witnesses are accepted according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
For Ramadan when cloudy, one witness suffices, even a mastur, female, slave, or repentant slanderer.

When the sky is obstructed by clouds or dust, the Hanafi school relaxes the usual requirements for witnessing the crescent moon. A single witness is accepted, and even persons of unknown character (mastur) or those who would be disqualified in other cases may testify. This leniency ensures that the community can start Ramadan without undue hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage applies when the sky is cloudy, dusty, or otherwise obstructed ('illa). This is the trigger for the relaxed rules.
2
Identify the first ruling
One 'adl (just) witness is accepted. ‘Adl means the person is known for righteousness, with good deeds outweighing sins.
3
Identify the refinement
The correct view accepts even a mastur (unknown status) witness. This is a relaxation compared to other testimonies.
4
Identify the further relaxation
Testimony on testimony (shahāda ‘alā shahāda) is accepted. The primary witness can be female, a slave, or even someone flogged for slander who repented.
5
Note procedural leniency
No formal wording like 'Ashhadu' is required, nor is a lawsuit needed. This is not a court proceeding but simply establishing the start of Ramadan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثبوت هلال رمضان إذا كان بالسماء علة :] وإذا كان بالسماء علة : من غَيْم، أو غبار، ونحوه: ١ - قبل خبر واحد عدل(٥). (٢) أو مستور، في الصحيح. ولو شَهِدَ على شهادة واحدٍ مثله (١) ولو كان أنثى، أو رقيقاً، أو محدوداً في قذف تاب: لرمضان. ولا يُشترط لفظ الشهادة، ولا الدعوى (٢).
Establishing the crescent of Ramadan when there is an obstruction in the sky: When there is an obstruction in the sky, such as clouds, dust, or the like: 1. The report of one just (adl) person is accepted. (2) Or an acceptable (mastur) person, according to the correct view. And if he testifies on the testimony of one like him, even if the witness is female, a slave, or one who was flogged for slander but has repented, (it is accepted) for Ramadan. And the wording of testimony is not required, nor the claim (lawsuit).
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage covers the conditions for accepting testimony to establish the crescent of Ramadan when the sky is not clear. Unlike other matters where two witnesses are needed, for Ramadan when cloudy, a single witness suffices. The witness must be 'adl (just, whose good deeds outweigh bad). If the witness is 'mastur' (unknown status, not known for sin or justice), some narrations accept him; the correct Hanafi position accepts him. Furthermore, testimony based on another's testimony (shahada 'ala shahada) is accepted for Ramadan even if the original witness is female, a slave, or even someone who was flogged for false accusation (qadhf) but has repented. Unlike regular legal testimony, no formal wording like 'Ashhadu' is required, and the matter need not be raised as a lawsuit in court.

Key Terms
العدل (al-'adl): A person whose good deeds outweigh his sins and who is known for fairness and avoiding major sins.
المستور (al-mastūr): A person whose condition is unknown (neither known for justice nor sin).
الشهادة على الشهادة (al-shahāda 'alā al-shahāda): Testifying based on another witness's testimony, i.e., a secondary witness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) One just man or one mastur (unknown) person
C) Only a free male
D) Anyone, including a non-Muslim

Key Takeaway
One just witness suffices to establish Ramadan crescent when the sky is cloudy, and the rules are relaxed compared to regular testimony.

Establishing Shawwal Hilal and the Dispute on Completion of Thirty Days
Lists conditions for Shawwal hilal and the ikhtilaf when month is completed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for establishing the Shawwal crescent and explain the internal Hanafi dispute regarding breaking fast when the month is completed to 30 days without sighting.

Core Idea
When the sky is clear, a large gathering is needed for both Ramadan and Shawwal; if only two witnesses confirm Ramadan, a dispute arises on whether the 30th day suffices for Shawwal.

This passage from Nur al-Idah outlines the fiqh rules for sighting the Shawwal crescent. It distinguishes between cloudy and clear skies, and then introduces a point of internal Hanafi disagreement: if Ramadan was confirmed by two just witnesses and the month reaches 30 days but the Shawwal hilal is not seen in clear sky, is it lawful to break fast? This lesson parses the conditions and the dispute, highlighting the difference of tarjih among Hanafi scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify conditions for cloudy sky
Locate the part of the text that specifies the requirement when the sky is cloudy: two free men or one free man and two free women, using the wording of testimony.
The text uses 'shurita' indicating a condition.
2
Identify conditions for clear sky
Find the requirement when the sky is not cloudy: a large gathering (jama' 'azim) is necessary, and its size is left to the imam's opinion.
The large gathering applies to both Ramadan and Shawwal, not just Shawwal.
3
Understand the first ruling (single witness case)
Note the scenario where Ramadan was established by a single witness, the month reaches 30 days, but no hilal is seen in clear sky: breaking fast is not permissible (la yahillu al-fitr).
This is a clear ruling without dispute.
4
Examine the dispute (two-witness case)
Read the fourth point: if Ramadan was established by two just witnesses, the month completes 30 days, but no hilal is seen in clear sky, there is a difference in tarjih (preferred view) among Hanafi scholars.
The text does not resolve the dispute; it simply notes the ikhtilaf.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Consider the practical consequence: depending on which view is followed, a community may either break fast on the 30th day or wait for sighting.
This dispute affects determining the start of Eid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[هلالُ الفطر :] وه ٢۷۱ ١ - وشرِطَ لهلال الفطر إذا كان بالسماء عِلَّةٌ: لفظ الشهادة من حُرَّيْن، أو حُرِّ وحُرتَيْن، بلا دعوى (۳) ٢ - وإذا لم يكن بالسماء عِلَّةٌ : فلا بدَّ من جَمْع عظيم لرمضان، والفطر. ومقدار الجمع العظيم : مفوض لرأي الإمام، في الأصح. وإذا تم العدد بشهادة فرد، ولم يُرَ هلالُ الفطر، والسماء مُصحِيَةٌ : لا يَحلُّ الفطر. ٤ـ واختلف الترجيح) فيما إذا كان بشهادة عدلين.
[Crescent of Shawwal:] 1. For the crescent of Shawwal, when the sky is cloudy, the condition is: the wording of testimony from two free men, or one free man and two free women, without a lawsuit. 2. When the sky is not cloudy, a large gathering is required for Ramadan and Shawwal. The measure of a large gathering is left to the imam's opinion, in the most sound view. 3. If the number (of days) is completed by the testimony of an individual and the crescent of Shawwal is not seen, and the sky is clear, breaking the fast is not permissible. 4. And there is a difference concerning the preferred view in the case when (the month is completed) by the testimony of two just witnesses.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage outlines the stricter conditions for establishing the crescent of Shawwal (Eid al-Fitr) compared to Ramadan. When the sky is cloudy, two free men or one free man and two free women are required, and they must explicitly use the wording of testimony (e.g., 'Ashhadu'). No lawsuit is needed. When the sky is clear, a large gathering (jama' 'azim) is necessary, and its size is left to the imam's discretion. If Ramadan was established by a single witness, but the crescent of Shawwal is not seen despite clear skies, fasting continues. The last point mentions a dispute: if the month was completed to 30 days based on the testimony of two just witnesses (i.e., Ramadan was confirmed by two witnesses), and the sky is clear but no hilal is seen, there is disagreement on whether breaking fast is permissible. The text does not resolve the dispute but hints at a tarjih issue.

Key Terms
الجمع العظيم (al-jam' al-'aẓīm): A large gathering of people who would testify that they saw the crescent; its exact number is determined by the imam.
الترجيح (al-tarjīḥ): The process of preferring one opinion over another when evidence conflicts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is required for establishing the crescent of Shawwal when the sky is cloudy?
A) One just witness
B) Two free men or one free man and two free women with the wording of testimony
C) A large gathering of any size
D) Astronomical calculation

Key Takeaway
The conditions for establishing Shawwal hilal depend on sky clarity, and when Ramadan was confirmed by two witnesses, completing 30 days without sighting leads to an unresolved Hanafi dispute on the permissibility of breaking fast.

Proving the Crescent of Ramadan and Adha with One Witness
Ruling on crescent sighting in cloudy conditions for Ramadan and Adha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that the crescent of Ramadan and Adha is established with one witness when the sky is cloudy, and explain the condition of celestial obstruction.

Core Idea
One witness suffices for Ramadan and Adha when the sky is cloudy.

This lesson covers the first ruling from the Book of Fasting regarding crescent sighting. The Hanafi school makes a concession for cloudy conditions, allowing the month to be established with a single witness. Understanding this rule is essential for correctly determining the start of Ramadan and Eid al-Adha.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
The text states that there is no dispute about breaking the fast when there is a celestial reason, even if Ramadan was proven by one witness. This applies to both Ramadan and Adha.
The ruling covers the crescent for both months.
2
Note the condition: celestial obstruction
The phrase 'إذا كان بالسماء علَّةٌ' (if there is a celestial reason) is the key condition. Without this condition, the normal requirement of two witnesses applies.
Examples of 'علة' include clouds, dust, or haze.
3
Understand the concession: one witness suffices
Under this condition, the testimony of a single upright individual is sufficient to prove the month. The word 'ولو' (even if) emphasizes that this holds even though normally two are required.
The witness must be just (ʿadl).
4
Recognize that there is no dispute on this point
The text says 'لا خلاف' (no dispute), indicating that other schools also agree on this concession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم
[تتمة :]
١ـ ولا خلاف في الفطر : إذا كان بالسماء علَّةٌ ولو (۱) ثَبَتَ رمضانُ بشهادة الفرد.
٢- وهلال الأضحى : كالفطر.
[ ثبوت بقية الأهلة : ]
ويُشترط لبقية الأهلة: شهادة رجلين عدلَيْن حُرَّيْن، أو حُرٌ وحُرتَيْن، غير محدودين في قذف
[ حكم اختلاف المطالع : ]
وإذا ثبت في مَطلَع قطرٍ: لَزِم سائر الناس، في ظاهر المذهب، وعليه الفتوى، وأكثر المشايخ.
[ رؤية الهلال نهاراً : ]
ولا عبرة برؤية الهلال نهاراً (۲) ، سواء كان قبل الزوال، أو بعده، وهو لليلة المستقبلة، في المختار.
*****
ير هلال شوال مع الصحو. مراقي.
(1) أي لا عبرة به من الليلة الماضية، بل لليلة المستقبلة. ط.
(۲) ولو وصلية.
Book of Fasting. Continuation: 1- There is no dispute about breaking the fast: if there is a celestial reason, even if Ramadan was proven by the testimony of one individual. 2- The crescent of Adha is like that of breaking the fast. [Proving other crescents:] For other crescents, it is required: testimony of two righteous free men, or one free man and two free women, not punished for false accusation. [Ruling on differing rising places:] When it is proven in the rising place of a region, it is binding on all people, in the apparent madhhab, and upon it is the fatwa, and the majority of the scholars. [Sighting the crescent during the day:] There is no consideration for sighting the crescent during the day, whether before or after noon, and it is for the coming night, in the chosen view. *** The crescent of Shawwal is seen with clear skies. Maraqi. (1) Meaning: there is no consideration for it from the previous night, but for the coming night. (2) Even if continuous.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a set of rulings on crescent moon sightings from Kitab al-Sawm. It begins by stating that there is no disagreement that it is permissible to break the fast (i.e., it is not obligatory to fast) when there is a celestial obstruction like clouds, even if the month of Ramadan was established by the testimony of a single witness. The same applies to the crescent of Eid al-Adha. For proving the beginning of other months (like Shawwal), the stronger requirement of two just free men or one man and two women is laid down. The next ruling addresses a key Hanafi position: if the crescent is sighted in one region, it becomes binding on all Muslims everywhere to act upon that sighting; this is the apparent madhhab and the view adopted for fatwa by most scholars. Finally, a crescent seen during the daytime – whether before or after noon – is not considered for the past night but is taken as the crescent for the coming night (i.e., the following night). The footnotes clarify that the daytime sighting is not counted for the night just past, and that the sighting is valid even if the sky is clear.

Key Terms
هلال (hilāl): The crescent moon at the beginning of a lunar month.
شهادة (shahāda): Testimony given before a qadi to establish an Islamic month.
علة (ʿilla): Celestial obstruction, such as clouds or dust, that hinders sighting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does one witness suffice to prove the crescent of Ramadan?
A) Always, because Ramadan is important.
B) Only when the sky is cloudy or there is a celestial reason.
C) Only when the witness is a woman.
D) Never, two witnesses are always required.

Key Takeaway
For Ramadan and Adha, one witness is sufficient when the sky is cloudy.

Requirements for Proving Other Months
Ruling on testimony requirements for proving months other than Ramadan and Adha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the required number and qualifications of witnesses for proving months other than Ramadan and Adha, and distinguish these from the rule for Ramadan/Adha.

Core Idea
For other months, two just free men or one man and two women are needed.

This lesson addresses the testimony requirements for months other than Ramadan and Adha, such as Shawwal and Muharram. The Hanafi school imposes a stricter standard, requiring either two male witnesses or one male and two female witnesses, all free and not guilty of qadhf (false accusation).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope: 'بقية الأهلة'
This includes all lunar months except Ramadan and the crescent of Adha (i.e., Dhul Hijja for Adha). The rule applies to months like Shawwal, Muharram, etc.
Adha is treated like Ramadan per the previous lesson.
2
State the minimum testimony: two men or one man and two women
The text requires 'شهادة رجلين عدلين حرين' (testimony of two righteous free men) OR 'حر وحرتين' (one free man and two free women). This is a higher standard than the one witness for Ramadan/Adha with a reason.
Women's testimony is accepted but must be paired.
3
Note the additional condition: 'غير محدودين في قذف'
Witnesses must not have been punished for qadhf (false accusation of fornication). Such punishment renders them unreliable for testimony.
This condition applies to all witnesses.
4
Differentiate from the previous rule
Contrast: For Ramadan/Adha with a celestial reason, one witness suffices. For other months, two men or one man and two women are required, and the sky condition is not mentioned.

The Binding Effect of Crescent Sighting Across Regions
Ruling on the global binding effect of crescent sighting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that crescent sighting in one region is binding on all Muslims, and identify that this is the apparent madhhab and majority view.

Core Idea
If the crescent is sighted in one region, it binds all Muslims everywhere.

This lesson addresses a key Hanafi principle: when the crescent moon is proven in one geographical region (maṭlaʿ), the ruling applies globally. This is the 'apparent madhhab' (zāhir al-madhhab) and the position adopted for fatwa by most Hanafi scholars. Understanding this helps explain why some Muslims may start Ramadan on different days.

Guided Path
1
State the rule: 'إذا ثبت في مطلع قطر: لزم سائر الناس'
The text says that when the crescent is proven in the rising place of a region, it is binding on all people. This means sighting in one locality obligates everyone to fast or break fast accordingly.
'مطلع' means the place where the moon rises, i.e., a geographical region.
2
Note that this is the apparent madhhab and fatwa position
The text qualifies: 'في ظاهر المذهب' (in the apparent madhhab) and 'وعليه الفتوى' (upon it is the fatwa). This indicates that while there is another view, the majority of scholars (أكثر المشايخ) prefer this one.
Other views exist, but this is the relied-upon position.
3
Understand the implied contradiction: differing rising places
The phrase 'اختلاف المطالع' (differing rising places) refers to the fact that the moon may appear in one region but not another due to latitude. The ruling says this difference does not affect the obligation.
This is the doctrine of ittiḥād al-maṭāliʿ (unity of rising places).
4
Recognize the practical implication
If the crescent is seen in Saudi Arabia, it is binding on Muslims in other countries to act on that sighting, even if they did not see it locally.
Some Hanafi scholars later relaxed this for distant regions.

Source Text

Ruling on Daytime Crescent Sighting
Ruling on the legal effect of daytime crescent sighting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a daytime sighting of the crescent is not considered for the past night but rather for the coming night, according to the chosen view.

Core Idea
A crescent seen during the day is for the coming night, not the past one.

This lesson clarifies what happens when the crescent moon is sighted during daylight hours. The Hanafi school rules that such a sighting is not retroactive; it establishes the month for the night to come. This avoids confusion about whether the previous night was the first of the month.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling: 'ولا عبرة برؤية الهلال نهاراً'
The text explicitly says there is no consideration for sighting the crescent during the day. This means it does not count for the night that has just passed.
Whether before or after noon, the ruling is the same.
2
Understand the reason: 'وهو لليلة المستقبلة'
The crescent seen during the day is assigned to the coming night. For example, if the crescent is seen after Fajr, the night that just ended is not part of the new month; the next night is.
The chosen view (في المختار) supports this.
3
Appreciate the practical effect
If someone sees the crescent during the day, they should not assume the previous night was the start of the month. The month actually begins with the coming night.
This prevents false starts of fasting or breaking fast.

What Does Not Invalidate Fasting: The Hanafi List
List of non-invalidating actions with commentary on forgetfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which actions do not invalidate the fast according to the Hanafi school and explain the ruling on reminding a forgetful person.

Core Idea
Forgetfulness preserves the fast; actions like looking, oil, and backbiting do not break it.

The Hanafi school lists twenty-four actions that do not invalidate the fast. This lesson covers the first eleven, focusing on the mercy of forgetfulness and the distinction between sin and nullification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first three acts and their shared condition
Eating, drinking, and intercourse are grouped under forgetfulness (nasiyan). The fast remains valid because the person did not intend to break it.
Forgetfulness is a mercy; the act is not counted as a breach.
2
Learn the ruling on reminding the forgetful person
If the forgetful person has strength (quwwah) to continue, the observer must remind him (obligatory). If weak, it is better not to remind, as the reminder may cause him to break his fast.
The footnote clarifies that 'karahah' here means prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi).
3
List the next acts that do not invalidate
These include: ejaculation by sight or thought, applying oil, using kohl, cupping, backbiting, intending to break without acting, and involuntary smoke inhalation.
Backbiting is a sin but does not nullify the fast. Oiling and kohl are permissible even if taste reaches the throat.
4
Understand the principle behind non-invalidating acts
The common thread: no substance enters the body intentionally through the natural orifice, nor does intentional sexual release occur. Involuntary entry or mental acts do not break the fast.
This aligns with Hanafi usul: the fast is only invalidated by deliberate consumption or intercourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما لا يُفسد الصوم
وهو أربعة وعشرون شيئاً:
١- ما لو أكل. ٢- أو شرب - أو جامع ناسياً). وإن كان للناسي قوة على الصوم: يُذكِّره (۳) به مَن رآه يأكل.
وكره (۳) عدم تذكيره.
وإن لم تكن له قوة: فالأولى عدم تذكيره.
٤ـ أو أنزل بنَظَر . ه أو فكر وإن أدام النظر والفكر.
٦- أو ادهن. أو اكتحل، ولو وَجَدَ طَعْمَه في حَلْقِه.
ه أو احتجم.
٩ أو اغتاب.
١٠ أو نوى الفطر ولم يُفطر.
١١ـ أو دخل حَلْقَه دخان بلا صنعه.
(۱) صومه.
(٢) أي لزوماً. ط.
(۳) أي تحريماً. ط.
What does not invalidate fasting. And it is twenty-four things: 1- If he eats, 2- or drinks, or has intercourse forgetfully. And if the forgetful person has strength to continue fasting: the one who sees him eating should remind him, and it is disliked (karahah) not to remind him. And if he does not have strength: then it is better not to remind him. 4- Or emits semen by look, 5- or thought, even if he continues the look and thought. 6- Or oils (himself), or applies kohl, even if he finds its taste in his throat. 8- Or is cupped. 9- Or backbites. 10- Or intends to break the fast but does not break it. 11- Or smoke enters his throat without his action. (1) His fast. (2) Meaning necessarily. (3) Meaning prohibitively disliked.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage enumerates actions that do not break the fast according to the Hanafi school. The title states there are 24 such things, but only 11 are listed here. The first three (eating, drinking, intercourse) are grouped together: if done forgetfully, the fast remains valid. However, if a person sees someone eating forgetfully, the ruling depends on the forgetful person's ability: if he is strong enough to continue fasting, it is obligatory (or recommended?) to remind him, and it is disliked (karahah tanzihiyyah? The footnote says 'prohibitively disliked' for the third footnote number, but that seems to apply to the 'karahah' of not reminding) not to remind. If he is weak, it is better not to remind him. Orgasm by looking or thinking does not invalidate the fast even if prolonged. Oiling the body and applying kohl do not break the fast even if the taste reaches the throat. Cupping, backbiting, intending to break the fast without actually doing so, and smoke entering involuntarily are all non-invalidating. The footnotes clarify that the fast remains valid in these cases and that certain actions are disliked or permissible.

Key Terms
ناسياً (nāsiyan): Forgetfully, without intentional remembrance of fasting.
قوة (quwwah): Strength to continue fasting after a forgetful action.
كره (kariha): Disliked (makruh), here applied to not reminding the forgetful person.
ادهن (id'han): To oil the body or hair.
اكتحل (iktahala): To apply kohl to the eyes.
احتجم (ihtajama): To undergo cupping (hijama).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a fasting person forgetfully eats and someone sees him, what should the observer do according to the lesson?
A) Always remind him immediately.
B) Never remind him.
C) Remind him if he has strength to continue; otherwise, do not remind.
D) Only remind him if he is weak.

Key Takeaway
Forgetfulness preserves the fast; many actions that are not eating, drinking, or intercourse do not invalidate even if sinful.

What Does Not Invalidate the Fast (Non-Invalidators)
Listing 20+ non-invalidators of the fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the actions that do not invalidate the fast according to the Hanafi school and explain why forgetful acts do not break the fast.

Core Idea
Forgetful eating, drinking, or intercourse does not break the fast; the fast remains valid.

This passage from Nur al-Idah enumerates twenty-four actions that do not invalidate the fast. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for every fasting Muslim to avoid unnecessary qada or kaffarah. We will examine the key rulings, especially the treatment of forgetfulness and unintentional actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal effect of forgetful acts
The fast remains valid (sahih) if a person eats, drinks, or has intercourse forgetfully. These acts are not considered breakers of the fast.
2
Determine the obligation to remind the forgetful person
If the forgetful person has strength to continue fasting, it is wajib (obligatory) to remind him. If he is weak, it is better not to remind him, lest he break his fast.
Reminding is wajib according to the text; not reminding is makruh tahrimi when strength is present.
3
Recognize that internal states do not break the fast
Wet dreams (whether from looking or thinking), applying oil or kohl, cupping, backbiting, and intending to break the fast without acting do not invalidate it.
These actions are not considered external intake or deliberate acts of eating/drinking.
4
Identify that unintentional entry of substances does not break the fast
Smoke, dust, flies, water entering the ear without deliberate action, and medicine taste in the mouth (if unintentional) do not invalidate the fast.
Intentional ingestion would break the fast; these are exceptions due to lack of intent.
5
Apply the ruling on janabah and similar states
Being in a state of janabah (major impurity) in the morning, even if it lasts all day, does not break the fast. Similarly, pouring water or oil into the urethra, or wading into a river so that water enters the ear, do not invalidate.
This is specific to Hanafi school; others may require ghusl before dawn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Book of Fasting. What does not invalidate the fast. And it is twenty-four things: 1. If he eats forgetfully. 2. Or drinks or has intercourse forgetfully. And if the forgetful person has strength to fast, whoever sees him eating should remind him. And it is disliked not to remind him. And if he does not have strength, then it is better not to remind him. 4. Or he has a wet dream due to looking. 5. Or thinking, even if he prolongs looking and thinking. 6. Or he applies oil or uses kohl, even if he tastes it in his throat. 7. Or he is cupped. 9. Or he backbites. 10. Or he intends to break the fast but does not break it. 11. Or smoke enters his throat without his doing. (1) His fast. (2) i.e., obligatorily. (3) i.e., prohibitively disliked. 12. Or dust, even dust from a mill (1). 13. Or a fly. 14. Or the trace of medicine taste in his mouth while he is mindful of his fast. 15. Or he becomes junub in the morning, even if he remains in janabah all day. 16. Or he pours water into his urethra. 17. Or oil. 18. Or he wades into a river, and water enters his ear (3). 19. Or he scratches his ear with a stick, and some dirt comes out, then he inserts it repeatedly into his ear. 20. Or mucus enters his nose, and he deliberately sniffs it and swallows it. Swallowing phlegm so that his fast is not invalidated according to Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him (5). (1) This is if he cannot avoid it; otherwise, he breaks fast. (2) i.e., in his throat while grinding medicine. (3) i.e., it enters by itself, and he did not put it in; and it will come on p. 282 that what invalidates the fast in the soundest view is if he puts drops in his ear. I said: And Al-Hidayah and its commentaries and Al-Walwalji chose non-invalidation absolutely, whether it enters by itself or he puts it in. (Tahatawi on Al-Maraqi p. 554). (4) i.e., it comes down from his head and reaches his nose. (5) i.e., considering the difference, so that his fast is valid by agreement; because it breaks the fast according to...
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah lists actions that do not invalidate the fast according to the Hanafi school. The author states there are twenty-four such things, but the text here enumerates twenty items and begins a note on swallowing phlegm. The first items cover forgetful eating, drinking, and intercourse. The ruling for a forgetful person differs: if he has strength to complete the fast, it is obligatory to remind him; if not, it is better not to remind. Subsequent items include wet dream from looking or thinking, which does not break the fast even if prolonged. Applying oil or kohl is permitted even if taste is felt. Cupping, backbiting, and intending to break the fast without actually doing so are also non-invalidating. Smoke and dust entering the throat without deliberate action, a fly, medicine taste in the mouth, being in a state of janabah, pouring water or oil into the urethra, water entering the ear without intent, scratching the ear with a stick, and deliberately sniffing mucus from the nose are all listed. The footnote on swallowing phlegm notes that according to Imam al-Shafi'i, it does not break the fast, and the Hanafi author recommends following this to avoid disagreement. The Hanafi position is that swallowing phlegm does break the fast, but here it mentions the Shafi'i view for those who wish to be cautious. The passage is a concise reference for what does not nullify the fast.

Key Terms
ناسياً (nāsiyan): Forgetfully; doing an action without remembering one is fasting.
أنزل بنَظَر (anzala bi-nazar): To have a wet dream or seminal emission due to looking (without physical contact).
إلقاء النخامة (ilqā' al-nukhāmah): Swallowing phlegm that comes from the nose or throat.
الجنابة (al-janābah): State of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The fast becomes invalid and must be made up with kaffarah.
B) The fast is still valid, and if he has strength, he should be reminded.
C) The fast is invalid, but no kaffarah is required.
D) The fast is valid, but he must stop immediately when reminded.

Key Takeaway
Forgetfulness does not break the fast; the fast remains valid and the forgetful person should be reminded if helpful.

Exceptions to Breaking the Fast: Dust, Insects, and Involuntary Entries (Items 12-20)
Enumerating exceptions with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which substances and actions from the passage do not invalidate the fast according to the Hanafi school, and apply the condition of unavoidability or involuntariness.

Core Idea
Not everything that enters the body breaks the fast; dust, flies, involuntarily water, and minimal traces are permitted.

The Hanafi matn lists specific cases where the fast remains valid despite something entering the body. These exceptions highlight the school's precise conditions: unintentional entry, unavoidable substances, or insignificant traces. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for correctly applying the rules of fasting in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Read each item attentively
Go through items 12-20 one by one, noting the exact wording and any conditions (e.g., 'if he cannot avoid it' for dust).
2
Identify the common principle
Determine why each item is exempted: either the entry is unavoidable, unintentional, or the substance is insignificant.
For example, dust and flies are difficult to avoid; water entering the ear is unintentional.
3
Note the Shafi'i difference on phlegm
The footnote recommends spitting out phlegm to avoid disagreement; Hanafi holds it does not break fast, Shafi'i does.
This is an example of observing khilaf for practical precaution.
4
Apply the conditions to new scenarios
Consider similar situations (e.g., inhaling dust while walking) and decide if they fall under the exemption or the general rule.
The key condition is whether the entry was intentional or avoidable.

(2) i.e., in his throat while grinding the medicine.
(3) i.e., it entered by itself, and he did not insert it. It will come on p. 282 that what invalidates the fast according to the soundest view is if he instills something in his ear. Commentary
This passage is from Nur al-Idah, a Hanafi matn, listing actions that do not invalidate the fast. Each numbered item is a distinct exception to the general rule that anything entering the body intentionally breaks the fast. The footnotes provide additional conditions and cross-references. For example, item 12 (dust) is subject to the condition that the dust cannot be avoided; if avoidable, it breaks the fast. Item 18 (water entering ear) specifies that it is by itself, not inserted; a later note indicates disagreement on induced entry. Item 20 (mucus) is followed by a separate note about phlegm: to avoid the Shafi'i view that swallowing phlegm breaks the fast, it is recommended to spit it out. Items 21-22 on vomiting: unintentional vomiting does not break the fast regardless of quantity, but intentional vomiting of less than a mouthful does not break according to the correct Hanafi position, though a narration holds it breaks and requires qada. Item 23: eating food stuck between teeth less than a chickpea is allowable. Item 24: chewing something like a sesame seed from outside the mouth until it dissolves without tasting it in the throat does not break the fast. The teacher should note that these exceptions are specific to the Hanafi madhhab and strengthen the student's understanding of the school's precise conditions for fasting validity.

Key Terms
الغبار (al-ghubar): Dust; item 12 exempts it if unavoidable.
الجنب (al-junub): Person in state of major impurity; does not invalidate fast.
النخامة (al-nukhamah): Phlegm; spitting recommended to avoid Shafi'i difference.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts from breaking the fast any unavoidable, unintentional, or insignificant entry of substances into the body.

Vomiting While Fasting: Involuntary vs. Intentional (Items 21-22)
Distinguishing between involuntary and intentional vomiting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between involuntary and intentional vomiting and state the Hanafi ruling for each regarding the validity of the fast.

Core Idea
Involuntary vomiting never breaks the fast; intentional vomiting only breaks if it is a mouthful or more.

Vomiting is a common concern for fasting persons. The Hanafi school makes a precise distinction: if vomiting is involuntary, the fast remains valid regardless of quantity. If intentional, the fast breaks only if the vomit fills the mouth. A lesser amount does not break, although some narrations require qada. Understanding this difference is essential for proper practice.

Guided Path
1
Define the two types of vomiting
Item 21 covers involuntary vomiting (ذَرَعَه), item 22 covers intentional vomiting (استقاء).
2
Apply the rule for involuntary vomiting
Even if the vomit returns by itself, the fast is not broken. The text says 'in the correct view'.
This is the relied-upon Hanafi position.
3
Apply the rule for intentional vomiting
If less than a mouthful, the fast is not broken; if a mouthful or more, it breaks and requires qada.
Footnote mentions a narration that even less than a mouthful breaks, but the correct view is as stated.
4
Consider the thamarah al-khilaf
The difference between the correct view and the outlier narration affects whether qada is required.
The mufta bihi is the correct view that less than a mouthful does not break.

19 - Or he scratches his ear with a stick, and dirt comes out on it, then he repeatedly inserts it into his ear.

Spitting out phlegm so that his fast is not invalidated according to Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him (5).

21 - Or vomiting overcomes him (1) and returns without his doing, even if it fills his mouth, according to the correct view.
22 - Or he deliberately vomits less than a mouthful, according to the correct view (3), even if he returns it, in the clear text [؟].
23 - Or he eats what is between his teeth, and it is less than a chickpea.
24 - Or he chews something like a sesame seed from outside his mouth until it dissolves, and he does not find its taste in his throat.

Footnotes:
(1) This is if he cannot avoid it; otherwise, he breaks his fast. [T.]
(2) i.e., in his throat while grinding the medicine.
(3) i.e., it entered by itself, and he did not insert it. It will come on p. 282 that what invalidates the fast according to the soundest view is if he instills something in his ear. I [the commentator] say: Al-Hidayah and its commentaries and al-Walwalji chose the opinion of no breaking of fast absolutely, whether it entered by itself or he inserted it. [Tahawi on al-Maraqi p. 554.]
(4) i.e., it descended from his head and reached his nose.
(5) i.e., observing the difference of opinion, so that his fasting is valid by agreement, for according to the Shafi'is, one who swallows phlegm breaks his fast. See Mughni al-Muhtaj 1/427.
(1) (from p. 275) i.e., it overcame him.
(2) (from p. 275) i.e., he deliberately caused vomiting.
(3) (from p. 275) The narration will come. p. 285 that this invalidates the fast and requires qada, and it is the apparent narration [؟].
The Shafi'is regarding swallowing phlegm see Mughni al-Muhtaj 1/427.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is from Nur al-Idah, a Hanafi matn, listing actions that do not invalidate the fast. Each numbered item is a distinct exception to the general rule that anything entering the body intentionally breaks the fast. The footnotes provide additional conditions and cross-references. For example, item 12 (dust) is subject to the condition that the dust cannot be avoided; if avoidable, it breaks the fast. Item 18 (water entering ear) specifies that it is by itself, not inserted; a later note indicates disagreement on induced entry. Item 20 (mucus) is followed by a separate note about phlegm: to avoid the Shafi'i view that swallowing phlegm breaks the fast, it is recommended to spit it out. Items 21-22 on vomiting: unintentional vomiting does not break the fast regardless of quantity, but intentional vomiting of less than a mouthful does not break according to the correct Hanafi position, though a narration holds it breaks and requires qada. Item 23: eating food stuck between teeth less than a chickpea is allowable. Item 24: chewing something like a sesame seed from outside the mouth until it dissolves without tasting it in the throat does not break the fast. The teacher should note that these exceptions are specific to the Hanafi madhhab and strengthen the student's understanding of the school's precise conditions for fasting validity.

Key Terms
القيء (al-qay'): Vomiting; distinguished between involuntary (ذَرَعَه) and intentional (استقاء).
ملء الفم (mil' al-fam): Mouthful; the threshold for intentional vomiting to break the fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person intentionally vomits less than a mouthful, the correct Hanafi view is:
A) The fast is broken and qada is required.
B) The fast remains valid, but qada is recommended.
C) The fast remains valid, and no qada is required.
D) The fast is broken only if he swallows it again.

Key Takeaway
Involuntary vomiting never breaks the fast; intentional vomiting only breaks if it is a mouthful or more.

Minimal Residues and External Chewing (Items 23-24)
Defining permissible small amounts and external chewing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating food stuck between teeth and chewing substances from outside the mouth while fasting.

Core Idea
Small residues between teeth (less than a chickpea) and chewing from outside until it dissolves without taste in throat do not break the fast.

Fasting persons may worry about food trapped between teeth or chewing gum. The Hanafi matn addresses these common situations: a small amount of leftover food (less than a chickpea) can be swallowed without breaking the fast. Similarly, chewing something like a sesame seed from outside the mouth, which dissolves without reaching the throat, is permissible. These exceptions show the school's detailed attention to daily fasting practice.

Guided Path
1
Item 23: Evaluate the quantity of food between teeth
If the leftover food is less than a chickpea, swallowing it does not break the fast. If it is a chickpea or more, it would break.
The chickpea is a customary measure.
2
Item 24: Understand the external chewing condition
Chewing something from outside the mouth (e.g., a seed) that dissolves without reaching the throat or leaving a taste is permissible.
The key is that no substance enters the throat.
3
Distinguish from intentional eating
These exceptions are for minimal, inadvertent ingestion; deliberate eating of food in normal amounts breaks the fast.

Four Cases Where the Fast Remains Valid
Continuation of list of invalidators requiring only qada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four scenarios in which the Hanafi school holds that the fast remains valid despite the entry of a substance into the stomach, and identify the condition of volition or quantity for each.

Core Idea
Unintentional vomit, small induced vomit, tiny food residue, and dissolving particles do not break the fast.

In the Hanafi school, not every substance that reaches the stomach invalidates the fast. This lesson covers four specific exceptions where the fast remains valid. Understanding these preserves the integrity of the fast while avoiding unnecessary qada. Each exception has a precise condition: involuntariness, small quantity, or lack of taste.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
Read items 21-24 carefully. Each describes an action: involuntary vomiting (21), intentional vomiting less than a mouthful (22), eating food residue between teeth less than a chickpea (23), and sucking a sesame-like particle from outside the mouth until it dissolves (24).
These are specific exceptions; the general rule is that intentional entry into the stomach breaks the fast.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
For each scenario, the ruling is the same: the fast remains valid (sahih). No qada or kaffarah is required. Confirm this from the text.
3
Note the qualifying condition
Each scenario has a condition: for vomit, it must be involuntary or less than a mouthful; for food residue, it must be less than a chickpea; for external particles, it must be sesame-sized and dissolve without reaching the throat.
If these conditions are not met (e.g., intentional vomiting of a mouthful, residue larger than chickpea), the fast is invalid and requires qada.
4
Contrast with invalidating cases
Recall the general invalidation: any substance that enters the stomach intentionally and in significant quantity breaks the fast. These four exceptions are cases where the entry is either involuntary, minimal, or without sensory detection.
This distinction is crucial for correct fatwa.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the list of actions that invalidate the fast but require only qada (make-up day), not kaffarah. Item 21: If a person is overcome by vomit and it returns to his stomach without his action, the fast is not broken even if it filled his mouth — this is the correct position in the school. Item 22: If a person intentionally induces vomiting but less than a mouthful, and even if he swallows it back, the fast remains valid according to the correct view. Item 23: Eating residue stuck between teeth that is less than a chickpea does not invalidate the fast. Item 24: Sucking a particle like a sesame seed from outside the mouth, such that it dissolves without reaching the throat, does not break the fast. These rulings are specific to the Hanafi school; the Shafi'i school may differ on some points, such as swallowing phlegm.

استقاء (istaqā'a): To intentionally induce vomiting.
الحمصة (al-ḥimmaṣa): A chickpea; used as a measure for the size of food residue.
مَضَعَ (maḍa'a): To suck or chew a solid substance without swallowing.
سِمْسِمَة (simsima): A sesame seed; used as a measure for a small particle.

B) The fast is invalid and requires only qada.
C) The fast remains valid.
D) The fast is invalid but no qada is required.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the fast remains valid in four specific exceptions: involuntary vomit return, intentional vomiting less than a mouthful, eating food residue smaller than a chickpea, and sucking a dissolved sesame-sized particle without tasting it.

Invalidators Requiring Qada and Kaffarah: Items 1–9
List of invalidators requiring both qada and kaffarah (items 1–9)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the first nine invalidators of the fast that require both qada and kaffarah, and state the general condition for their applicability.

Core Idea
Nine specific acts invalidate the fast and require both qada and kaffarah if done intentionally without compulsion.

This passage introduces a list of twenty-two actions that, if performed while fasting intentionally and voluntarily, not only break the fast but also require expiation (kaffarah) along with making up the day (qada). Here we cover the first nine items, ranging from intercourse to eating wheat. The common condition is that the act must be done willfully, not under duress. Memorizing these foundational rulings is essential for every Hanafi student.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category and condition
Recognize that these are mufsidat (invalidators) that trigger both qada and kaffarah. The general condition is that the act is done voluntarily (طائعاً), intentionally (متعمداً), and without compulsion (غير مضطر).
If any of these conditions is missing, only qada may be required, not kaffarah.
2
Memorize items 1–4
Item 1: Intercourse in either front or back passage – both parties incur qada and kaffarah. Items 2–3: Eating and drinking, whether nourishing or medicinal. Item 4: Swallowing rainwater that enters the mouth intentionally.
Even if the rainwater enters accidentally, if one intentionally swallows it, it counts.
3
Memorize items 5–7
Item 5: Raw meat, even if putrid, except if it is wormy (then it is not considered food). Item 6: Fat (by the choice of Abu al-Layth). Item 7: Dried meat (by scholarly agreement).
The exception for wormy meat shows that spoiled, non-nutritive substances may not invalidate.
4
Memorize items 8–9
Item 8: Eating whole wheat grains. Item 9: Cracking (chewing) wheat grains, unless one chews a single grain without swallowing until it dissolves – then it does not invalidate.
The exception for dissolving a grain without swallowing indicates that mere taste without ingestion is not an invalidator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يُفسد الصوم وتجب به الكفارة مع القضاء
وهو اثنان وعشرون شيئاً، إذا فعل الصائم شيئاً منها طائعاً، متعمداً، غير مُضطَرِّ : لَزِمَه القضاء والكفارة:
١- الجماع في أحد السبيلين، على الفاعل، والمفعول به.
٢- والأكل.
٣- والشرب، سواء فيه ما يتغذى به، أو يُتداوى به.
٤- وابتلاع مطر دخل في فَمِه.
٥ - وأكل اللحم النّي وإن كان منتناً، إلا إذا دَوَّد.
٦- وأكلُ الشَّحْم ، في اختيار الفقيه أبي الليث.
٧- وقديد اللحم، بالاتفاق.
٨- وأكل الحنطة.
٩- وقضمها ، إلا أن يَمضَعَ قَمْحةً، فتلاشت.
Chapter on what invalidates the fast and requires kaffarah along with qada. It is twenty-two things: when the fasting person does any of them voluntarily, intentionally, without compulsion, qada and kaffarah become incumbent upon him: 1- Intercourse in either of the two passages, upon the doer and the one acted upon. 2- Eating. 3- Drinking, including what is used for nourishment or medication. 4- Swallowing rainwater that entered his mouth. 5- Eating raw meat, even if putrid, unless it is wormy. 6- Eating fat, according to the choice of al-Faqih Abu al-Layth. 7- Dried meat, by agreement. 8- Eating wheat. 9- Cracking it, unless he chews a grain and it dissolves.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage opens a new section listing the invalidators (mufsidat) of the fast that require both qada (making up the day) and kaffarah (expiation). The list contains twenty-two items; only the first nine are given on this page. The common condition is that the act is done willfully, intentionally, and without duress. Item 1: Intercourse in either the front or back passage invalidates the fast and requires both qada and kaffarah for both parties. Item 2-3: Eating and drinking, whether nourishing or medicinal. Item 4: Swallowing rainwater that enters the mouth accidentally (if done intentionally it also invalidates). Item 5: Raw meat, even if putrid, but not if it is wormy (as it is then considered spoiled and not normal food). Item 6: Eating fat — this is according to the choice of Imam Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi, a prominent Hanafi jurist. Item 7: Dried meat, by scholarly agreement. Items 8-9: Eating whole wheat grains or cracking them (chewing) invalidates, unless one simply chews a grain until it dissolves without swallowing it. These rulings are specific to the Hanafi school and should be memorized as foundational.

Key Terms
الكفارة (al-kaffārah): Expiation required for invalidating the fast with certain acts; includes freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor people.
القضاء (al-qaḍā'): Making up the missed fast on another day.
الجماع (al-jimā'): Sexual intercourse, specifically penetrating the front or back passage.
اللحم النّي (al-laḥm al-niy): Raw meat, uncooked.
الشَّحْم (al-shaḥm): Animal fat.
القديد (al-qadīd): Dried or jerked meat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is required if a person intentionally eats dried meat while fasting?
A) Only qada is required.
B) Only kaffarah is required.
C) Both qada and kaffarah are required.
D) Neither is required.

Key Takeaway
The first nine invalidators requiring both qada and kaffarah include intercourse, eating, drinking, swallowing rainwater, raw meat (unless wormy), fat (by Abu al-Layth), dried meat, wheat, and cracking wheat (unless chewed and dissolved).

The Twenty-Two Invalidators Requiring Qada and Kaffarah
Listing the 22 invalidators of fasting with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the 22 invalidators of fasting that require both qada and kaffarah, and identify which exceptions apply.

Core Idea
Twenty-two specific acts nullify the fast and require both makeup and expiation when done intentionally and voluntarily.

This lesson covers the classic Hanafi list of actions that break the fast and necessitate both qada (makeup) and kaffarah (expiation). The list includes sexual intercourse, eating, drinking, and various other acts. Understanding these invalidators is essential for correctly identifying when a fast is nullified and what the required penalty is.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the first category: intercourse and consumption
Identify items 1-3: intercourse in either passage, eating, and drinking (including medicinal and rainwater). These are the primary invalidators.
Intercourse applies to both active and passive participant.
2
Learn the detailed eating items (4-14)
List includes raw meat (except if wormy), fat, dried meat, wheat grains, biting grains (unless dissolved), swallowing a grain or sesame seed, Armenian earth, other earth if habitual, and small salt.
Exceptions: wormy meat only qada; large salt no kaffarah.
3
Understand the saliva and post-acts categories (15-21)
Items 15-21 cover swallowing spouse's or friend's saliva, and eating after backbiting, cupping, touching, kissing with desire, or lying together without ejaculation.
Saliva of others besides spouse/friend does not nullify.
4
Recognize the underlying conditions
All 22 invalidators require the act to be done willingly (ta'a), intentionally (mut'ammadan), and voluntarily without coercion.
If forced, only qada is required, not kaffarah.
5
Differentiate between qada-only and qada+kaffarah cases
The passage notes exceptions where only qada is due: wormy meat, chewing a grain until it dissolves, and large salt. Most items incur both.
This distinction is crucial for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يُفسد الصوم وتجب به الكفارة مع القضاء

كتاب الصوم

وهو اثنان وعشرون شيئاً، إذا فعل الصائم شيئاً منها طائعاً، متعمداً، غير مُضطَرِّ : لَزِمَه القضاء والكفارة:

- الجماع في أحد السبيلين، على الفاعل، والمفعول به.

٢- والأكل.

والشرب، سواء فيه ما يتغذى به، أو يُتداوى به.

وابتلاع مطر دخل في فَمِه.

٥ - وأكل اللحم النّي وإن كان مُنتناً، إلا إذا دَوَّد). ٦- وأكلُ الشَّحْم ) ، في اختيار الفقيه أبي الليث(۳).

Chapter: What invalidates fasting and for which atonement is obligatory along with makeup. The Book of Fasting. It is twenty-two things: when the fasting person does any of them willingly, intentionally, and not forced, then makeup and atonement are incumbent upon him: (1) Intercourse in either of the two passages, on the doer and the one to whom it is done. (2) Eating. (3) Drinking, both what nourishes and what is used as medicine. (4) Swallowing rainwater that entered his mouth. (5) Eating raw meat even if smelly, except if it has become wormy. (6) Eating fat, according to the choice of Abu al-Layth. (7) Dried meat, by agreement. (8) Eating wheat grains. (9) Biting them, unless he chews a single grain and it dissolves. (Footnote: No atonement then, because it exits from being nourishment). (10) Swallowing a grain of wheat. (11) Or a sesame seed or similar from outside his mouth, in al-Mukhtar. (12) Eating Armenian earth absolutely. (13) And other than Armenian, like a child if accustomed to eating it. (14) A small amount of salt, in al-Mukhtar. (15) Swallowing the saliva of his wife. (16) Or of his friend, not others. (17) Eating intentionally after backbiting. (18) Or after cupping. (19) Or after touching. (20) Or a kiss with desire. (21) Or after lying together without ejaculation.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah lists the twenty-two actions that nullify a fast and require both qada (makeup) and kaffarah (expiation) in the Hanafi school. The ruling applies when the fasting person does any of these acts voluntarily, intentionally, and without coercion. The first item is sexual intercourse in either the front or back passage—this incurs expiation on both the active and passive participant. Eating and drinking follow, including both nourishing and medicinal substances, as well as swallowing rainwater that entered the mouth. Raw meat is included even if putrid, but if it becomes wormy (i.e., spoiled beyond edibility), no kaffarah is required. The fat of meat is included per the authoritative choice of Imam Abu al-Layth, while dried meat is agreed upon. Eating whole wheat grains or biting them incurs kaffarah, except if one chews a single grain and it dissolves entirely—then only qada is due. Swallowing a single wheat grain or a sesame seed brought from outside the mouth is included in al-Mukhtar. Eating Armenian earth (a type of clay) is unconditionally invalidating, and other earth is included if one habitually eats it. A small amount of salt similarly incurs kaffarah according to al-Mukhtar. Swallowing the saliva of one's spouse or a friend (but not others) nullifies the fast. Finally, eating after backbiting, after cupping, after touching, after a kiss with desire, or after lying together without ejaculation are all listed as invalidators. The passage includes important clarifications: if the meat is wormy, no kaffarah; if salt is consumed in large quantity, no kaffarah (as it is not a normal food); and the definition of Armenian earth is provided. This list is a classic memorization anchor for students of Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation: a prescribed penalty for nullifying a fast, such as fasting sixty consecutive days or feeding sixty poor persons.
قضاء (qaḍāʾ): Makeup: fasting another day to replace the invalidated fast.
جماع (jimāʿ): Sexual intercourse, specifically the act of penetration in either passage.
صوم (ṣawm): Fasting: abstaining from intentional eating, drinking, and sexual activity from dawn to sunset with intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following requires only qada and not kaffarah?
A) Eating raw meat that has become wormy
B) Swallowing the saliva of one's wife
C) Eating a large amount of salt
D) Biting a wheat grain without swallowing it

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school enumerates 22 specific invalidators that necessitate both qada and kaffarah when committed intentionally and voluntarily.
