Exceptions to Kaffarah Obligation for Breaking the Fast
Exceptions to kaffarah for breaking the fast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the exceptions where kaffarah is not required for breaking the fast and apply the condition that knowing the interpretation of a hadith triggers obligation.

Core Idea
Ignorance or reliance on a legitimate fatwa can remove the obligation of expiation for breaking the fast.

This passage from Nur al-Idah clarifies when expiation (kaffarah) for breaking the fast in Ramadan is excused. It addresses situations where a person follows a fatwa from another madhhab or hears a hadith without knowing its interpretation according to the Hanafi school. Understanding these exceptions prevents imposing kaffarah wrongly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling.
Recall that kaffarah is normally wajib for deliberately breaking the fast by intercourse or eating/drinking without valid excuse.
2
Recognize the first exception: fatwa from a faqih.
If a person acts on a fatwa from a qualified jurist (e.g., a Hanbali ruling that cupping breaks the fast), no kaffarah is required, even if the Hanafi school disagrees.
The fatwa must be from a recognized madhhab or scholar; mere personal opinion does not suffice.
3
Recognize the second exception: hearing a hadith without knowing its interpretation.
If someone hears a hadith that seems to indicate breaking the fast (e.g., 'the cupper and the cupped break the fast') but does not know the Hanafi interpretation that the hadith is abrogated or weak, no kaffarah is due.
The key is lack of knowledge of the school's interpretation.
4
Note the condition that triggers kaffarah: knowledge of interpretation.
If the person knows the interpretation (e.g., the hadith is abrogated), then kaffarah becomes wajib upon breaking the fast based on that act.
This prevents using ignorance as a blanket excuse.
5
Identify the case of coercion.
A woman who willingly submits to coercion (intercourse) during the day in Ramadan incurs kaffarah, but if genuinely coerced, it is not obligatory.
The text states 'on the one who submits', implying willingness nullifies the excuse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(١) أي من الأمور المتقدمة من غيبة أو حجامة.... (٢) أي حنبلي مثلاً، إذ الحجامة عند الحنابلة تفطر. ينظر الفروع ٤٧/٣. Or if he oils his moustache thinking that he breaks the fast by that (1). Except: The Book of Fasting (2) a. If a legal expert gave him a fatwa (3) b. Or he heard the hadith, and does not know its interpretation according to the school (4). And if he knows its interpretation (5), then expiation is obligatory upon him. Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah states rulings on when expiation (kaffarah) for breaking the fast is dropped or obligatory. The author lists exceptions: if a person follows a faqih's fatwa (e.g., a Hanbali ruling that cupping breaks the fast) or hears a hadith but does not know its interpretation according to the Hanafi school, then no expiation is due. However, if he knows the interpretation (e.g., that the hadith is abrogated or weak), then expiation is wajib. Similarly, expiation is obligatory on a woman who willingly submits to coercion (intercourse) during the day in Ramadan. The footnotes clarify the evidence and sources, including the hadith 'The cupper and the cupped break the fast' and its status, as well as the weakness of the hadith on backbiting breaking the fast. The overall point is that ignorance or reliance on a legitimate fatwa can remove the obligation of expiation.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation required for deliberately breaking the fast of Ramadan without valid excuse.
فتوى (fatwā): A legal opinion given by a qualified jurist (faqih).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) If he follows the fatwa of a Hanbali faqih.
D) If he oils his moustache thinking it breaks the fast.

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah is not required when a person breaks the fast based on a faqih's fatwa or a hadith they cannot interpret, but becomes wajib if they know the correct interpretation.

Kaffarah: When It Drops and Its Three Options
Defines when expiation drops and lists three alternatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions that cause expiation to drop and list the three options of expiation in their strict sequential order according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Expiation drops only by events beyond control — menstruation, postpartum, or illness — not by forced travel; its alternatives are strictly sequential.

This passage from Nur al-Idah explains two crucial aspects of kaffarah (expiation) for breaking the fast. First, it clarifies when the obligation of expiation is lifted after being due. Second, it details the three-tiered structure: freeing a slave, fasting two consecutive months, or feeding sixty poor people. Understanding both is essential for correctly applying this wajib act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dropping conditions
The passage lists three events that cause kaffarah to drop: menstruation, postpartum bleeding, and illness that permits breaking the fast on that day. Note that forced travel does not drop it.
These are events that occur after the obligation is due.
2
Understand the non-dropping case
The text explicitly states that forced travel after obligation does not drop kaffarah in the apparent narration (fi zahir al-riwayah).
Travel, even if involuntary, does not remove the obligation.
3
Learn the sequential alternatives
The first option is freeing a slave (even a non-Muslim slave). If unable, then fasting two consecutive months excluding Eid and Tashriq days. If unable, then feeding sixty poor people a filling meal.
The order is mandatory: one cannot skip to feeding unless unable to fast.
4
Recognize the exclusions in fasting
The two consecutive months must not include Eid al-Fitr, Eid al-Adha, or the days of Tashriq (11-13 Dhul Hijjah), as fasting on these days is prohibited.
If such days occur during the two months, they break the consecutiveness and must be counted separately.
5
Apply the sequence in practice
When performing kaffarah, one must start with the first option and move down only if unable. This is a strict sequential obligation.
Inability is determined by Shari'ah criteria, not personal preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصوم فصل في الكفارة، وما يُسقطها عن الذمة سقوط الكفارة، ولزومها :] ١- تسقط الكفارة: أ - بطرو حيض. ۲۷۹ ب ـ أو نفاس ج ـ أو مرض مبيح للفطر في يومه. ٢- ولا تسقط عمن سُوفِرَ به كُرْهاً بعد لزومها عليه، في ظاهر الرواية. والكفارة: ١- تحرير رقبة، ولو كانت غير مؤمنة. ٢- فإن عَجَزَ عنه (۱) : صام شهرين متتابعين، ليس فيهما يوم عيد، ولا أيام التشريق. فإن لم يستطع الصوم: أ - أطعم ستين مسكيناً، يُغَدِّيهم، ويُعَشيهم (۳) غداء وعشاء مُشبِعَيْن، (1) أي عن التحرير. (۲) هم أنفسهم الذين غداهم.
The Book of Fasting. Chapter on expiation, and what removes it from liability. Dropping of expiation and its necessity: 1. Expiation drops: a. by occurrence of menstruation; b. or postpartum bleeding; c. or illness that permits breaking the fast on that day. 2. And it does not drop from one who is forced to travel after it has become obligatory upon him, in the apparent narration. And the expiation: 1. Freeing a slave, even if not a believer. 2. If he is unable to do that (1): he fasts two consecutive months, in which there is no day of Eid, nor the days of Tashriq. If he cannot fast: a. He feeds sixty poor people, giving them a filling lunch and dinner (3). (1) i.e. unable to free a slave. (2) They are themselves those whom he fed.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah details the rulings on expiation (kaffarah) for breaking the fast of Ramadan. It first lists three conditions that cause the obligation of expiation to drop after it was due: the onset of menstruation, postpartum bleeding, or illness that permits breaking the fast on that day. However, expiation does not drop for one who is forced to travel after the obligation was established (according to the apparent narration). Then the passage explains the expiation itself: it is three-tiered. The primary obligation is freeing a slave (even a non-Muslim slave). If unable, one must fast two consecutive months, excluding Eid days and days of Tashriq. If unable to fast, one feeds sixty poor people a filling meal (lunch and dinner). The footnote clarifies that 'freeing' refers to the inability to free a slave. This is the Hanafi position on the progressive alternatives for kaffarah.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation consisting of three options: freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor.
أيام التشريق (ayyām al-tashrīq): The 11th, 12th, and 13th of Dhul Hijjah, when fasting is prohibited.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The expiation for breaking the fast drops only in three specific situations; its performance follows a strict sequence of options.

Kaffarah for Breaking Fast and Rules of Overlap
Ruling statement on kaffarah for breaking fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions that drop the obligation of kaffarah and apply the rule of overlap for multiple expiations.

Core Idea
One expiation suffices for multiple violations if no expiation was performed in between.

This lesson explains the expiation (kaffarah) required when someone deliberately breaks a Ramadan fast by sexual intercourse. The text outlines three circumstances that cancel the obligation, the graded forms of expiation, and the critical rule on overlapping expiations. Understanding these rules ensures that the expiation is performed correctly and not needlessly repeated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act that triggers kaffarah
The kaffarah is for deliberately invalidating a Ramadan fast by sexual intercourse. Note that it does not apply to eating or drinking.
2
Recognize conditions that drop the kaffarah
The text lists three events (menstruation, postnatal bleeding, illness permitting break) that occur on the same day and cause the kaffarah obligation to fall away.
These events occur before the kaffarah is performed, not after it becomes binding.
3
Memorize the graded sequence of expiation
The kaffarah has three forms in strict order: freeing a slave, then fasting two consecutive months, then feeding sixty poor. Each step is only allowed if the previous is impossible.
The slave need not be Muslim; the fast excludes Eid and Tashreeq days; feeding has specific quantities.
4
Apply the overlap rule
If multiple acts of intercourse occur on different days without any expiation performed in between, one kaffarah suffices for all. If expiation was performed between acts, each violation requires its own kaffarah.
The 'correct opinion' (sahih) is that one kaffarah covers multiple violations without intervening expiation.

Source Text

كتاب الصوم

في الكفارة، وما يُسقطها عن الذمة

أ - بطرو حيض.
ب ـ أو نفاس

٢- ولا تسقط عمن سُوفِرَ به كُرْهاً بعد لزومها عليه، في ظاهر الرواية.
والكفارة:
١- تحرير رقبة، ولو كانت غير مؤمنة.
٢- فإن عَجَزَ عنه (۱) : صام شهرين متتابعين، ليس فيهما يوم عيد، ولا أيام التشريق.
فإن لم يستطع الصوم:
أ - أطعم ستين مسكيناً، يُغَدِّيهم، ويُعَشيهم (۳) غداء وعشاء مُشبِعَيْن،أو غداءين(۱)، أو عشاءَيْن، أو عشاء وسُحوراً.

[تداخل الكفارات :]
١ - وكَفَتْ كفارةٌ واحدةٌ عن جماع (۳) متعدد في أيام لم يتخلله تكفير، ولو من رمضانين، على الصحيح.
٢ـ فإن تخلل التكفير : لا تكفي كفارة واحدة، في ظاهر الرواية.
Book of Fasting. Chapter on the expiation, and what removes it from liability. Fall of expiation and its obligation: 1. Expiation falls away due to: a) onset of menstruation, b) or postnatal bleeding, c) or illness that permits breaking the fast on that day. 2. And it does not fall away from one who is forced to travel (coerced) after it has become obligatory upon him, according to the apparent narration. And the expiation: 1. Freeing a slave, even if not a believer. 2. If unable to do so: fast two consecutive months, in which there is no day of Eid nor the days of Tashreeq. If unable to fast: a) Feed sixty poor persons, giving them a filling lunch and dinner, or two lunches, or two dinners, or dinner and suhoor. b) Or give each poor person half a sa' of wheat, or its flour, or its roasted barley, or a sa' of dates or barley, or its value. [Overlap of expiations]: 1. One expiation suffices for multiple acts of intercourse on days not interrupted by expiation, even if from two Ramadans, according to the correct opinion. 2. If expiation was performed in between, one expiation does not suffice, according to the apparent narration.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from Nur al-Idah on the topic of kaffarah (expiation) for intentionally breaking the fast of Ramadan by sexual intercourse. It first lists conditions under which the obligation of kaffarah falls away: if the person menstruates, experiences postnatal bleeding, or falls ill in a way that permits breaking the fast on that same day. However, if the kaffarah has already become obligatory (i.e., after committing the violation), later coercion to travel does not remove it. The text then details the three graded forms of kaffarah, which must be performed in order: freeing a slave (any slave, even non-Muslim), fasting two consecutive months (excluding Eid and Tashreeq days), and feeding sixty poor persons with a filling meal or giving a specified quantity of staple food or its value. Finally, it covers the overlap of multiple kaffarahs: if a person commits intercourse on multiple days without having performed expiation in between, a single kaffarah suffices for all. But if he performed expiation after one violation, then committed another, he must do another expiation. The relied-upon (sahih) view is the first case; the second is from the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya).

Key Terms
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation required for deliberately invalidating a Ramadan fast by sexual intercourse; consists of freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor.
التكفير (at-takfir): The act of performing the expiation (e.g., freeing a slave, fasting, or feeding).
ظاهر الرواية (zahir ar-riwayah): The transmitted rulings from Imam Muhammad's six books, considered authoritative in the school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under which condition does the obligation of kaffarah fall away after it has become binding?
A) If the person is forced to travel.
B) If the person falls ill the next day.
C) If the person menstruates on that same day.
D) If the person forgets to perform the expiation.

Key Takeaway
One expiation suffices for multiple violations of intercourse during Ramadan fasting, provided no expiation was performed in between.

Tadakhul al-Kaffarat: When One Expiation Suffices
Ruling on when multiple expiations merge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two scenarios in Hanafi law where one kaffarah suffices for multiple violations versus when separate kaffarat are required and apply the ruling.

Core Idea
One expiation covers multiple violations until expiation is performed; after that, each violation requires a new expiation.

The principle of tadakhul (merging) of expiations in Hanafi fiqh determines how many kaffarat are due when multiple invalidating acts occur. This lesson clarifies when a single kaffarah suffices and when multiple are necessary, based on whether expiation was performed in between. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly fulfilling obligations after fasting violations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is intercourse during a Ramadan fast, which requires kaffarah (wajib). The passage specifies the number of kaffarat required for multiple occurrences.
Kaffarah is a specific expiation: freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
Scenario A: Multiple violations occur without performing expiation in between. Scenario B: Expiation is performed after a violation, then another violation occurs.
3
Apply the principle of tadakhul
In Scenario A, one kaffarah suffices for all violations, even spanning two Ramadans, according to the soundest view. In Scenario B, tadakhul does not apply; a new kaffarah is required for the subsequent violation.
The reasoning is that the obligation of kaffarah is a single liability until it is fulfilled.
4
Note the authority levels
Scenario A is 'ala al-sahih' (the soundest view). Scenario B is the ruling in Zahir al-Riwayah (authoritative transmissions). Both are relied upon.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the soundest view for Scenario A.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[تداخل الكفارات :]
١ - وكَفَتْ كفارةٌ واحدةٌ عن جماع متعدد في أيام لم يتخلله تكفير، ولو من رمضانين، على الصحيح.
٢ـ فإن تخلل التكفير : لا تكفي كفارة واحدة، في ظاهر الرواية.
[Interference of Expiations:] 1. One expiation suffices for multiple instances of intercourse on days not separated by expiation, even if from two Ramadans, according to the soundest view. 2. If expiation intervened, one expiation does not suffice, in the apparent transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah).
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the interference (tadakhul) of multiple expiations in fasting. It addresses whether a person who commits multiple violations requiring kaffarah (like intercourse during Ramadan) must perform multiple expiations. The ruling distinguishes two scenarios: (1) If the violations occur on different days and expiation has not been performed in between, then one expiation suffices for all, even if the violations span two Ramadans. This is the soundest (asahh) position in the school. (2) If expiation was performed after a violation and then another violation occurs, a separate expiation is required for the new violation. This is the ruling according to Zahir al-Riwayah, the authoritative transmissions of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. The reasoning is that the obligation of expiation is a single liability that is fulfilled once; subsequent violations before fulfillment are covered, but after fulfillment a new obligation arises.

Key Terms
تداخل الكفارات (tadakhul al-kaffarat): The legal principle of whether multiple expiations are merged or separate.
على الصحيح (ala al-sahih): According to the soundest position in the school.
في ظاهر الرواية (fi zahir al-riwayah): In the apparent (authoritative) transmissions of the school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Two expiations, one for each day.
B) One expiation suffices for both days.
C) No expiation required, only qada.
D) Depends on the school of the person.

Key Takeaway
One kaffarah covers multiple violations until expiation is performed; after that, each new violation requires its own kaffarah.

Items That Invalidate the Fast Without Expiation
List of items breaking fast without expiation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the nine items listed in Nur al-Idah that break the fast and require only qada, and apply the Hanafi principle that distinguishes between substances requiring kaffarah and those requiring only qada.

Core Idea
Eating non-food or inedible items invalidates the fast but only requires qada, not kaffarah.

This passage from Nur al-Idah lists nine specific items that, if eaten by a fasting person, invalidate the fast but do not require expiation (kaffarah). Understanding this list helps apply the Hanafi rule that kaffarah is only for substances normally consumed as food or drink. The list includes raw rice, dough, flour, a large amount of salt, certain clays, date pits, cotton, paper, and unripe uncooked quince.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common thread
All these items are not normally eaten as food or drink. The Hanafi school distinguishes between 'food' (which requires kaffarah) and 'non-food' (which requires only qada).
The general principle is that anything that is not typically consumed by humans only necessitates qada.
2
Memorize the nine items
Use a mnemonic: Rice, Dough, Flour, Salt, Clay, Pit, Cotton, Paper, Quince. Group them: Grains (rice, dough, flour), inorganic (salt, clay), fibrous (pit, cotton, paper), fruit (quince).
Practice recalling them regularly.
3
Apply the distinction
If a fasting person eats any of these items intentionally, their fast is invalidated and they must make it up (qada) but no kaffarah. If they eat a normal food like bread or dates, both qada and kaffarah are required.
Exception: if the person habitually eats clay (like Armenian clay), then kaffarah applies.

٢ ـ أو عجيناً.

٦- أو نواة.

Chapter: What invalidates fasting and necessitates qada (makeup). And it is fifty-seven things: 1. If the fasting person eats raw rice. 2. Or dough. 3. Or flour. 4. Or a large amount of salt at once. Without expiation. 5. Or clay that is not Armenian, which he is not accustomed to eating. 6. Or a date pit. 7. Or cotton. 8. Or paper (kaaghid). 9. Or quince that is not yet ripe and not cooked.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement listing items that invalidate the fast (mufsidat al-sawm) but only require qada (makeup) without kaffarah. The Hanafi school distinguishes between substances that are normally consumed as food/drink (which require both qada and kaffarah) and those that are not. The items listed here—raw rice, dough, flour, a large amount of salt, non-Armenian clay (unless habitual), date pits, cotton, paper, and unripe uncooked quince—are not considered food or drink, so only qada is due. The text notes 'from without expiation' explicitly. The footnote clarifies that if one is accustomed to eating clay (e.g., Armenian clay), then kaffarah is required. Also, for salt, if taken in multiple small sips, the first sip might require both qada and kaffarah, but this passage addresses a single large amount. The list originally states 57 items, but only these nine are mentioned here.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): Makeup of a missed or invalidated fast by fasting another day.
من غير كفارة (min ghayr kaffara): Without expiation; these invalidations only require qada, not kaffarah.
سَفَرْجَل (safarjal): Quince; unripe and uncooked quince is inedible.

Actions That Invalidate the Fast in Hanafi Fiqh
List of mufsidat (nullifiers) of the fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general principle for what invalidates the fast and classify given actions as requiring qada' only or qada' with kaffarah.

Core Idea
Anything entering the body through an open cavity invalidates the fast; kaffarah is an exception, not the rule.

The fast is a sacred act of worship, and its validity depends on abstaining from more than just food and drink. This lesson presents a comprehensive list from Nur al-Idah of 27 actions that nullify the fast and necessitate making it up (qada'). Understanding this list helps you grasp the underlying principle: anything that reaches the interior (jowf) via an open orifice breaks the fast. We'll also see when expiation (kaffarah) becomes necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
The list is built on one core idea: if something enters the body through the mouth, nose, ears, or other open cavity and reaches the interior, the fast is broken and qada' is wajib. Kaffarah only applies in specific cases mentioned in the footnotes.
Look for items 1-14 (solids), 15-21 (medical procedures), 22-26 (liquids/forced).
2
Categorize the listed actions
Group items into: (a) edible/non-edible solids (rice, clay, paper, etc.), (b) medical interventions (enema, snuff, ear drops, wound treatment), (c) liquids (rainwater, mouthwash water), (d) coerced actions. This reveals the breadth of nullifiers beyond eating and drinking.
Item 23 (snow) and 24 (mouthwash) have special conditions.
3
Distinguish qada' from kaffarah
Most items only require qada'. The footnotes specify that kaffarah is added when the substance is customary food eaten intentionally (e.g., habitual clay, multiple salt intakes). Memorize: kaffarah is for intentional consumption of normal foodstuffs, not for unusual items or medical procedures.
See footnotes (1) and (2) for examples.
4
Note exceptions and clarifications
The last note (from al-Hidayah) states that if water or snow enters the throat by itself (not deliberately swallowed), the fast is not broken. This refines the principle: intention matters.
Contrast item 22 (rainwater) with the note: deliberate swallowing invalidates, accidental does not.
5
Apply the principle to new scenarios
Now that you know the principle, test yourself: Does an injection into the arm break the fast? (No, because it does not enter through an open cavity.) Does swallowing a vitamin pill break it? (Yes, qada' only, no kaffarah unless it is customary food.)
Always check the route of entry and the nature of the substance.

Chapter of Fasting: Section on What Invalidates the Fast and Obligates Making It Up. There are fifty-seven things: 1 - If the fasting person eats raw rice. 2 - Or dough. 3 - Or flour. 4 - Or a lot of salt at once (1), without expiation. 5 - Or non-Armenian clay that he is not accustomed to eating (2). 6 - Or a date pit. 7 - Or cotton. 8 - Or paper (3). 9 - Or unripe and uncooked quince. (1) If it was in small amounts: then with the first amount, qada' and expiation are obligatory. (2) If he is accustomed to it: then expiation is obligatory. (3) It means paper and similar things not normally eaten. 10 - Or a fresh walnut. 11 - Or he swallows a pebble. 12 - Or iron. 13 - Or dust. 14 - Or a stone. 15 - Or he receives an enema. 16 - Or he uses snuff. 17 - Or something is poured into his throat (the correct view). 18 - Or he drips oil into his ear. 19 - Or water (the correct view). 20 - Or he treats a penetrating wound. 21 - Or he treats a head wound with medicine that reaches his interior or his brain. 22 - Or rainwater enters his throat. 23 - Or snow (the correct view), and he did not swallow it deliberately. 24 - Or he breaks his fast mistakenly due to water from rinsing his mouth reaching his interior. 25 - Or he breaks his fast under coercion, even by intercourse. 26 - Or she is coerced into intercourse. Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah presents a list of actions that invalidate the fast (mufsidat) and require qada' (making up the fast). The author states there are fifty-seven items but lists only twenty-seven here. The general principle is that anything that enters the interior of the body (jowf) through an open cavity (mouth, nose, ears, etc.) intentionally and with knowledge of being in a state of fasting breaks the fast and necessitates qada'. Expiation (kaffarah) is not required for these items unless specified in the notes (e.g., when salt is taken in many small amounts, or when someone is accustomed to eating clay). Items 1–14 cover various solid substances that are not normally eaten. Items 15–20 cover medical procedures like enemas, snuff, pouring oil into the ear, etc. Items 21–26 cover liquids or forced actions. The last note clarifies that if something enters the throat by itself (like rainwater or snow) it does not break the fast according to al-Hidayah, unless deliberately swallowed. This list serves as a detailed reference for students to memorize the many nullifiers of the fast.

Key Terms
ما يفسد الصوم (ma yufsid al-sawm): Actions that nullify the validity of the fast, requiring qada' and possibly kaffarah.
القضاء (al-qada'): Making up the missed fast on another day after Ramadan.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for intentionally breaking the fast; mentioned in notes for specific cases.
الجوف (al-jawf): The interior of the body; entry of anything through an open cavity to the jowf invalidates the fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The fast is not broken because it is not food.
C) The fast is broken and kaffarah is required.
D) The fast becomes makruh but still valid.

Key Takeaway
The fast is invalidated by any substance that deliberately enters the interior through an open cavity; qada' is always wajib, while kaffarah is only for intentional consumption of normal food.

Invalidators of the Fast (Mufsidat al-Sawm)
Enumeration of thirty-four acts that break the fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the thirty-four acts that invalidate the fast according to the Hanafi school and explain the legal principle that something entering the body's interior (jowf) intentionally breaks the fast.

Core Idea
Fasting is invalidated when any substance enters the body's interior intentionally, even if not food or drink.

This lesson presents the comprehensive list of invalidators (mufsidat) from Nur al-Idah. You will learn to identify each act and understand the underlying principle of jowf (body cavity). Pay attention to the exceptions for forgetfulness and coercion.

Guided Path
1
Categorize the invalidators by type
Group the 34 items into categories: ingestion of foreign objects (10-14), medical interventions (15-21), accidental entries (22-24), coerced acts (25-27), acts while sleep/forgetful (28-30), and intention-related acts (31-34). This helps memory and reveals the common pattern.
Not all categories are mutually exclusive.
2
Identify the common principle
Most invalidators involve something reaching the interior of the body (jowf) through a natural or created passage, intentionally. Even if the substance is not normally nutritive (e.g., pebble, dust), it still invalidates.
Exceptions: self-induced vomiting is debated, but not in this list.
3
Distinguish qada from kaffarah
In Hanafi fiqh, intentional eating/drinking/intercourse requires both qada and kaffarah. Most other invalidators require only qada. Note that items 25-26 (coercion) require qada only; item 30 (intentional intercourse after forgetful) might require both.
The passage does not explicitly state kaffarah; this is supplementary Hanafi rule.
4
Note the exceptions and nuances
Forgetful eating does not invalidate (hadith in footnote 3). If one mistakenly thinks he broke his fast and then eats intentionally, it does invalidate (item 29). Coercion does not remove the invalidating effect but avoids sin.
The hadith is a key proof.
5
Apply to hypothetical scenarios
Given a new case (e.g., swallowing a pill, using an asthma inhaler), check if it involves intentional entry into jowf. Use the list as a benchmark to determine if it falls under the same principle.
This develops fiqh judgment.

Mughni al-Muhtaj 1/423. (5) And he had intended fasting at night and did not break his resolve. Maraqi. (6) Having intended from the night. (7) So he missed the condition of validity, so his fast is not valid, and he owes only qada.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is a list of acts that invalidate the fast (mufsidat al-sawm) from the matn of Nur al-Idah. It begins with item 10 (indicating the previous items are elsewhere). The list covers a wide range: swallowing foreign objects (walnut, pebble, iron, dust, stone), administering enemas, snuff, pouring liquids into the throat or ear, treating deep wounds, rain or snow entering the throat accidentally, water from rinsing going down, being forced to break fast, coerced intercourse, a woman breaking fast out of fear of illness from service, being fed while asleep, eating intentionally after having eaten forgetfully, intercourse after forgetful intercourse, eating without a valid intention due to missing tabyit al-niyyah or changing residency status, and refraining without any intention. The footnotes clarify: footnote (1) on the first page notes a cross-reference that if water enters by itself, it does not break the fast according to al-Hidayah. The second footnote on page 283 defines 'fear' as based on strong probability, not mere illusion. Footnotes (2)-(4) explain the ruling on forgetful eating: the hadith shows that forgetfulness does not invalidate; but if one mistakenly thinks he broke his fast and then eats intentionally, it is a problem. Footnotes (4)-(7) discuss intention: the Hanafi view is that tabyit al-niyyah (overnight intention) is a condition for validity; if missing, the fast is invalid and only qada is required, not kaffarah. The student should memorize these invalidators to know what actions require qada (and possibly kaffarah) when done intentionally.

Key Terms
مفطر (mufṭir): An act that invalidates the fast, requiring qada (and sometimes kaffarah).
تبييت النية (tabyīt al-niyyah): Making the intention for fasting the night before, before dawn.
جوف (jawf): Body interior/cavity; invalidators are acts that cause something to reach the jawf intentionally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following does NOT invalidate the fast?
A) Swallowing a pebble intentionally
B) Having water enter the nose while rinsing
C) Being forced to have intercourse
D) Making intention during the day without overnight intention

Key Takeaway
Fasting is invalidated by any intentional entry of a substance into the body cavity, and the Hanafi list of 34 invalidators serves as a comprehensive guide for qada and kaffarah.

Nullifiers of the Fast (Items 27-48)
Listing 22 actions that nullify the fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for each of the 22 nullifiers listed and identify which actions require only qada' and which may involve additional compensation.

Core Idea
Any action that causes the fast to be invalid requires qada', even if done mistakenly or under doubt.

Fasting is an act of worship whose validity depends on avoiding specific invalidators. This lesson presents a detailed list of 22 scenarios from items 27 to 48 of Nur al-Idah, covering everything from fear of illness to sexual acts. Understanding these nullifiers helps you distinguish between actions that break the fast and those that do not, ensuring your worship remains sound.

Guided Path
1
Scan the list
Read through items 27 to 48 quickly to get an overview of the types of nullifiers: those related to eating/drinking, intercourse, emission, and other actions.
Pay attention to footnotes; they clarify conditions like predominant conjecture.
2
Categorize each nullifier
For each item, identify whether it involves deliberate intent, forgetfulness, doubt, or a specific condition (e.g., being asleep, being a traveler).
Many nullifiers are based on doubt or incorrect assumption, not just intentional acts.
3
Apply the ruling
For each item, determine if it invalidates the fast (requiring qada') and if any additional penalty (kaffarah) is mentioned. The text generally implies just qada' unless stated otherwise.
Only items involving deliberate intercourse during Ramadan typically require kaffarah; here most are simply qada'.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Item 34 and footnotes mention the Shafi'i requirement of night intention. Understand that this list is based on the Hanafi position, and some items may be ruled differently in other schools.
This difference affects whether a fast is considered valid at all if intention was not made at night.
5
Memorize the key patterns
Focus on recurring themes: doubt about dawn/sunset, intentional actions after forgetfulness, and emission from various stimulations.
Use the list as a mnemonic: actions that break the fast are those that involve ingestion, intercourse, or deliberate act contrary to the spirit of fasting.

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah continues the enumeration of actions that invalidate the fast (mufsidat al-sawm). The author lists items 27 to 48, covering various scenarios involving doubt, forgetfulness, coercion, and sexual acts. The footnotes provide important clarifications: for instance, 'fear' is based on predominant conjecture (ghalabat al-zann), not mere illusion. The hadith about eating forgetfully is cited to show that forgetful eating does not break the fast, but if one then eats intentionally, the fast is broken. The footnote about the intention requirement notes a difference with the Shafi'i school, who require night intention for obligatory fasts. The list includes actions like eating after having intended during the day without night intention, traveling and then eating, refraining without intention, eating the pre-dawn meal (suhur) after dawn? Actually 'tashahhara' likely means ate suhur, which if done after dawn could break fast. The text then covers intercourse in doubt of dawn, breaking fast assuming sunset while sun still up, emission of semen by intercourse with a dead woman or animal, or by thighing, kissing, touching, spoiling a non-Ramadan fast (e.g., a voluntary fast), being penetrated while asleep, inserting something into the vagina, inserting a moist finger into the anus, and a woman inserting a moist finger into her vagina. Each item is a separate legal case where the fast is invalidated and qada' is required.

Key Terms
أفطر (afṭara): To break the fast intentionally or by a factor that invalidates it.
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-ẓann): Predominant conjecture based on strong probability, not mere illusion.
ناسياً (nāsiyan): Forgetfully, i.e., without intent to break the fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) His fast is valid because he acted on his assumption.
B) His fast is invalid and he must make it up.
C) He must continue fasting if he realizes before sunset.
D) He must make up the fast and pay kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
The fast is invalidated by a specific set of actions, most requiring only qada', and each case is determined by the intention and circumstances.

Sexual and Timing-Related Nullifiers of Fasting
Lists sexual and timing-related nullifiers of fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the Hanafi rulings on nullifiers involving sexual activity and mistaken belief about dawn or sunset.

Core Idea
Sexual arousal and mistaken timing break the fast even without eating or drinking.

Fasting requires abstinence from far more than just food and drink. The Hanafi school enumerates specific acts that nullify the fast, including those related to sexual activity and errors about the time of day. This lesson covers the rulings on intercourse with doubt, orgasm through various means, and breaking the fast due to mistaken timing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act type
Each item specifies a scenario: intercourse with doubt about dawn, breaking fast after sunset yet sun remains, and various ways of achieving orgasm (with corpse, animal, thighing, pressing thighs, kissing, touching). Also includes being penetrated while asleep.
These all involve either doubt about time or sexual arousal.
2
Note the ruling for each
All listed actions break the fast and require qada (make-up). The fast becomes invalid immediately upon commission.
No distinction between intentional and unintentional for these specific acts.
3
Understand the logic
The school considers any sexual contact that leads to orgasm as breaking the fast, regardless of the object (including corpse or animal). Similarly, intercourse with doubt about dawn is treated as if it occurred after dawn, thus nullifying.
The ruling on mistaken sunset (item 37) is analogous: if you break fast thinking it is sunset but sun is still up, the fast is invalid.
4
Memorize the list
These are core memorization points for Hanafi fiqh. Use the numbering to recall each item.
Focus on the patterns: doubt about time, direct intercourse, indirect orgasm, and being penetrated while asleep.

(2) Ibn ʿĀbidīn transmitted in 6/189-190 from a group of imams of the school the correction of making it obligatory to make up, and he said: The result is that there are two corrected statements and the relied-upon view is the obligation of making up because it is the apparent narration and the texts.

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of a ruling list enumerating the actions that invalidate the fast (mufsidāt al-ṣawm) in the Hanafi school. The author, Qudūrī or the commentator, presents items 35–57, each providing a specific scenario that breaks the fast. The list begins with eating after the time for suhoor (pre-dawn meal) has passed, then intercourse with doubt about dawn, breaking fast thinking it is sunset while the sun remains, and various forms of sexual activity leading to orgasm (with a corpse, animal, thighing, pressing thighs, kissing, touching). Invalidating other non-Ramadan voluntary fasts is included. For women, being sexually penetrated while asleep or inserting medicine into the vagina is invalidating. Inserting a moist finger into the anus or vagina, inserting a cotton ball, or deliberately inhaling smoke are all nullifiers. Induced vomiting: the apparent narration (Ẓāhir al-Riwāya) says any amount breaks the fast, but Abu Yusuf requires a mouthful, which is considered the correct view. Swallowing back vomit a mouthful while mindful, eating residue between teeth the size of a chickpea, making intention after eating forgetfully, losing consciousness for even a full month (but no make-up for the day it occurred or the night before), and non-continuous insanity for a full month are also listed. The footnote clarifies that making up for the unconscious day is debated; the relied-upon view is obligation, as it is the Ẓāhir al-Riwāya position. Each ruling is a straightforward enumeration without proof, intended for memorization by students of the madhhab.

Key Terms
تسحر (tasaḥḥara): Not directly in this lesson, but related to time.
شاكاً (shākkan): Doubting; having uncertainty about the time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The fast is invalid and both qada and kaffarah are required.
D) The fast is valid because the sun was not yet visible.

Key Takeaway
Actions leading to orgasm, intercourse with doubt about dawn, and breaking fast assuming sunset while sun remains are all nullifiers that require make-up.

Insertion and Inhalation Nullifiers of Fasting
Lists insertion and inhalation nullifiers of fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the nullifiers involving insertion of objects into the body and deliberate inhalation of smoke.

Core Idea
Inserting anything into the body cavity or inhaling smoke intentionally breaks the fast.

The fast is not only broken by what enters the mouth; the Hanafi school rules that inserting any object (finger, cotton, medicine) into the private parts or anus, or deliberately inhaling smoke, nullifies the fast. The threshold for insertion is the amount of an enema, which rarely occurs. This lesson explains items 46-51.

Guided Path
1
Categorize the actions
Items 46-51 involve insertion into vaginal opening, anus, or throat. Item 46: inserting medicine into vagina. Items 47-48: inserting a moist finger into anus or vagina. Items 49-50: inserting a cotton ball. Item 51: inhaling smoke deliberately.
These are actions that bypass the mouth but still introduce substances into the body.
2
Note the quantity threshold
The margin note (2) states that the threshold for nullification via insertion is the amount of an enema (ḥuqna), which is a significant amount rarely reached. So a small amount of medicinal liquid may not break the fast.
This is a practical concession: most medical suppositories or minor insertions do not break the fast.
3
Understand the chosen views
Item 46 says 'according to the most correct view' (ʿalā al-aṣaḥḥ), indicating that there is a weaker view that it does not break the fast. Item 48 says 'according to the chosen view' (fī al-mukhtār).
These show that the school prefers the stricter view on these insertions.
4
Apply to modern scenarios
Modern equivalents: inserting a medical swab, vaginal cream applicator, or using an inhaler (if smoke-like). However, the threshold of ḥuqna gives leniency for small amounts.
Consult a qualified mufti for specific medical procedures.

35. Or he took suhoor (ate the pre-dawn meal).
36. Or he had intercourse while doubting the dawn, and it had risen.
37. Or he broke the fast thinking it was sunset, but the sun was still present.
38. Or he had an orgasm by intercourse with a corpse.
39. Or with an animal.
40. Or by thighing.
41. Or by pressing thighs together.
42. Or by kissing.
43. Or by touching.
44. Or he invalidated a fast other than making up Ramadan.
45. Or she was slept with while sleeping.
46. Or she inserted [medicine] into her vagina, according to the most correct view.
47. Or he inserted his finger, moistened with water or oil, into his anus.
48. Or she inserted it into her internal vagina, according to the chosen view.
(1) By overwhelming assumption.
(2) The threshold amount for nullification when inserted is the amount of an enema, which rarely occurs. [Marāqī]
(3) i.e., her finger moistened with water or oil. [Marāqī] I note that the word 'finger' is masculine and feminine. [Mukhtār al-Ṣiḥāḥ, al-Miṣbāḥ al-Munīr]
49. Or he inserted a cotton ball into his anus and concealed it.
50. Or into her internal vagina and concealed it.
51. Or he inserted smoke into his throat deliberately.
52. Or he induced vomiting, even if less than a mouthful, according to the apparent narration; Abu Yusuf conditioned it being a mouthful, and this is correct.
53. Or he swallowed back the vomit that had risen, if it was a mouthful, while mindful of his fast.
54. Or he ate food stuck between his teeth, if it was the size of a chickpea.
55. Or he intended fasting during the day after eating forgetfully before making his intention for the day.
56. Or he became unconscious, even for an entire month, except that he does not have to make up the day on which the unconsciousness occurred, or if it occurred during its night.
57. Or he became insane for a non-continuous entire month.
The correct view: He is not required to make it up if he regains consciousness at night or during the day after the time for intention has passed. (2)
(1) Refer to p. 275; this was mentioned earlier, and the author considered it there as not breaking the fast, and that is the correct view. (2) Ibn ʿĀbidīn transmitted in 6/189-190 from a group of imams of the school the correction of making it obligatory to make up, and he said: The result is that there are two corrected statements and the relied-upon view is the obligation of making up because it is the apparent narration and the texts.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of a ruling list enumerating the actions that invalidate the fast (mufsidāt al-ṣawm) in the Hanafi school. The author, Qudūrī or the commentator, presents items 35–57, each providing a specific scenario that breaks the fast. The list begins with eating after the time for suhoor (pre-dawn meal) has passed, then intercourse with doubt about dawn, breaking fast thinking it is sunset while the sun remains, and various forms of sexual activity leading to orgasm (with a corpse, animal, thighing, pressing thighs, kissing, touching). Invalidating other non-Ramadan voluntary fasts is included. For women, being sexually penetrated while asleep or inserting medicine into the vagina is invalidating. Inserting a moist finger into the anus or vagina, inserting a cotton ball, or deliberately inhaling smoke are all nullifiers. Induced vomiting: the apparent narration (Ẓāhir al-Riwāya) says any amount breaks the fast, but Abu Yusuf requires a mouthful, which is considered the correct view. Swallowing back vomit a mouthful while mindful, eating residue between teeth the size of a chickpea, making intention after eating forgetfully, losing consciousness for even a full month (but no make-up for the day it occurred or the night before), and non-continuous insanity for a full month are also listed. The footnote clarifies that making up for the unconscious day is debated; the relied-upon view is obligation, as it is the Ẓāhir al-Riwāya position. Each ruling is a straightforward enumeration without proof, intended for memorization by students of the madhhab.

Key Terms
حقنة (ḥuqna): Enema; the threshold amount for insertion to nullify the fast.
إدخال (idkhāl): Insertion; act of putting something into the body cavity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whether the substance is medicine.
B) The amount inserted, specifically the amount of an enema.
C) Whether the insertion is voluntary.
D) Whether the substance is liquid.

Key Takeaway
Insertion nullifies the fast only if the amount reaches that of an enema; deliberate smoke inhalation always breaks it.

Oral and Gastric Nullifiers: Vomiting, Residue, and Suhoor Timing
Lists oral nullifiers including vomiting, residue, and suhoor timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rulings on induced vomiting, swallowing back vomit, eating residual food, and eating suhoor after Fajr.

Core Idea
Induced vomiting breaks the fast, but the amount is disputed between any amount and a mouthful.

Beyond food and drink, the Hanafi school details other oral nullifiers: eating suhoor after Fajr, induced vomiting, swallowing back vomit, and eating food stuck between teeth. This lesson covers items 35 and 52-54, including the intra-madhhab dispute on the required amount of vomit to break the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions
Actions: eating suhoor after Fajr (35), induced vomiting (52), swallowing back vomit that reached the mouth (53), and eating food residues between teeth that are the size of a chickpea (54).
All involve oral intake or expulsion.
2
Understand the vomiting dispute
Item 52: 'according to the apparent narration (Ẓāhir al-Riwāya), any amount, even less than a mouthful, breaks the fast.' Abu Yusuf conditioned it being a mouthful, and this is considered the correct view (al-ṣaḥīḥ).
The relied-upon view (muftā bihī) is the mouthful condition.
3
Apply the chickpea criterion
Item 54: eating food residue between teeth is a nullifier if the amount is the size of a chickpea (ḥimmaṣa). Smaller amounts are excused.
This is a practical guideline for everyday eating.
4
Compare with unintentional swallowing
If vomiting occurs involuntarily or residue is swallowed without choice, the fast does not break. The key is mindfulness (dhikr) and deliberateness.
Item 53 explicitly mentions 'while mindful of his fast' (huwa dhākirun li-ṣawmihī).

Nullifiers Related to Intention, Consciousness, and Other Situations
Lists nullifiers related to intention and consciousness states

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the ruling on fasting when a person invalidates a non-Ramadan fast, makes intention after forgetful eating, becomes unconscious, or becomes insane.

Core Idea
Unconsciousness and insanity break the fast, but the obligation to make up is disputed.

The final items in the list deal with unusual situations: invalidating a non-Ramadan fast (intentionally breaking a voluntary fast), making the intention for fasting after having eaten forgetfully, and losing consciousness or sanity. A footnote indicates a later dispute on whether unconsciousness requires make-up. This lesson covers items 44, 55-57 and the associated intra-madhhab discussion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actions
Item 44: invalidating a fast that is not the performance of Ramadan (i.e., a voluntary fast or a make-up fast). Item 55: making the intention for fasting during the day after having eaten forgetfully before intending. Item 56: unconsciousness (ighmāʾ) for even the entire month. Item 57: non-continuous insanity (junūn) for the entire month.
These are not related to time or insertion but to the state of the person.
2
Understand the ruling on unconsciousness
Item 56: Unconsciousness for even the entire month breaks the fast, but there is an exception: 'except that he does not have to make up the day on which the unconsciousness occurred, or if it occurred during its night.' This is the initial statement. The footnote (2) cites Ibn ʿĀbidīn who notes a dispute: the relied-upon view is that qada is required, as per Ẓāhir al-Riwāya.
The text itself says 'except that he does not have to make up', but the footnote clarifies that the muftā bihī is the opposite.
3
Understand the ruling on insanity
Item 57: 'Or he became insane for a non-continuous entire month.' Meaning the insanity must span the entire month but not necessarily continuously? The phrase 'ghayr mumtadd' (non-continuous) means it occurs within the month but not every day? Actually it means the insanity is not extended throughout the whole month? The text says 'non-continuous entire month' – likely meaning that he was insane for a period that lasted a full month, but not necessarily consecutive. The ruling: it breaks the fast.
The footnote (2) adds that the correct view is that he does not have to make it up if he regains consciousness after the time for intention has passed.
4
Apply the rules
For item 55: if a person forgetfully eats before making the intention for the day, then later makes the intention, the fast is still valid? Actually item 55 says 'he intended fasting during the day after eating forgetfully before his intention' – this breaks the fast? The text is ambiguous: 'aw nawa al-ṣawma nahāran baʿda mā akala nāsiyan qabla ījādi niyyatihi min al-nahār' – means he ate forgetfully during the day before making intention, and then made intention later. The ruling is that this breaks the fast? Actually the commentary is needed. Since it's in the list, it indicates this action nullifies the fast: because eating forgetfully before intention is not acceptable if the intention is made after. In the Hanafi school, the intention must be made before dawn for obligatory fasts; for voluntary fasts, it can be made before midday. If he ate after dawn, then intended a voluntary fast, the fast is broken because he already ate. So this is a nullifier.
The intention after forgetful eating nullifies because the act of eating, even if forgetful, breaks the fast if it occurs before the intention for a voluntary fast.

Invalidators of Fasting: Insertions, Vomiting, and Consciousness
List of invalidators with intra-Hanafi disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules on invalidators of fasting listed in the text and distinguish the relied-upon positions on vomiting and unconsciousness/insanity.

Core Idea
Insertions, induced vomiting, and unconsciousness break the fast; but vomiting requires a mouthful and unconsciousness onset day is not made up.

This passage from Nur al-Idah lists actions that invalidate fasting, from insertion of objects to loss of consciousness. While most rulings are straightforward, the text highlights a key intra-Hanafi dispute: does induced vomiting invalidate regardless of amount or only if a mouthful? Also, unconsciousness and insanity raise questions about qada (make-up). Mastering these nuances ensures your practice of fasting is precise and in line with the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify each invalidator
Read items 49-57 and list each action that invalidates the fast. Note that some involve intentional insertion (rectum, vagina, throat), others involve vomiting or eating food between teeth, and others involve mental states (unconsciousness, insanity).
Memorise the list as a unit.
2
Examine the vomiting dispute
Item 52 says that according to Zahir al-Riwayah, vomiting even less than a mouthful invalidates, but Abu Yusuf required a mouthful, and that is the correct (sahih) view. Understand the practical difference: if a fasting person intentionally vomits less than a mouthful, the fast remains valid per the relied-upon view.
This is a clear intra-Hanafi tarjih.
3
Analyse unconsciousness and insanity rulings
Item 56 states that unconsciousness invalidates the fast, but the day it began or the night it occurred is not made up. Item 57 initially says insanity does not require qada, but the footnote from Ibn Abidin corrects this: the relied-upon view is that qada is required because it is the Zahir al-Riwayah. Note the shift in authority.
The text includes a later correction (Ibn Abidin) that overrides the initial 'sahih'.
4
Apply the rulings to scenarios
Practice: If a person intentionally inserts a cotton ball into their rectum, the fast is invalid (item 49). If they vomit a mouthful and swallow it back while remembering the fast, it invalidates (item 53). If they become unconscious for the entire month, they must make up all days except the first day/night (item 56 per the relied-upon view).
The practical effect of the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is limited to the amount of vomit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤٩ـ أو أدخل قطنةٌ في دُبُره، وغيبها. ٥٠ أو في فَرْجِها الداخل، وغيبتها. ٥١ـ أو أدخل حلقه دخاناً بصنعه. ٥٢ـ أو استقاء، ولو دونَ مِلء الفم، في ظاهر الرواية)، وشَرَطَ أبو يوسف ملء الفم، وهو الصحيح. ٥٣ أو أعاد ما ذَرَعَه من القيء، وكان ملء الفم، وهو ذاكر لصومه. ٥٤ أو أكل ما بين أسنانه، وكان قَدْرَ الحمصة. ٥٥ أو نوى الصوم نهاراً بعد ما أكل ناسياً قبل إيجاد نيته من النهار. ٥٦ـ أو أغمي عليه، ولو جميع الشهر، إلا أنه لا يقضي اليوم الذي حَدَث فيه الإغماء، أو حَدَثَ في ليلته. ٥٧ أو جُنَّ غير مُمتد جميع الشهر. الصحيح ولا يلزمه قضاؤه بإفاقته ليلاً أو نهاراً بعد فوات وقت النية، في (١) وينظر ص٢٧٥ فقد تقدم هذا، وعده المصنف هناك مما لا يُفسد الصوم، وأنه هو الصحيح. (٢) نقل ابن عابدين ١٨٩/٦ - ١٩٠ عن طائفة من أئمة المذهب تصحيح لزوم قضائه، وقال : الحاصل أنهما قولان مصححان وأن المعتمد لزوم القضاء؛ لكونه ظاهر الرواية والمتون.
49. Or he inserted a cotton ball into his rectum and hid it. 50. Or into her internal vagina and hid it. 51. Or he introduced smoke into his throat by his own action. 52. Or he induced vomiting, even if less than a mouthful, according to the apparent transmission, but Abu Yusuf conditioned a mouthful, and that is correct. 53. Or he returned what he brought up of vomit, and it was a mouthful, while he was mindful of his fast. 54. Or he ate what was between his teeth, and it was the size of a chickpea. 55. Or he intended the fast during the day after having eaten forgetfully before making his intention in the daytime. 56. Or he became unconscious, even for the entire month, except that he does not make up the day on which the unconsciousness occurred, or it occurred in its night. 57. Or he became insane non-continuously for the entire month. The correct (view): he is not required to make it up upon regaining consciousness by night or by day after the time of intention has passed. (1) See page 275; this has preceded, and the author counted it there among things that do not invalidate fasting, and that it is correct. (2) Ibn Abidin, 6/189-190, quoted from a group of imams of the school the correction of the necessity of making it up, and he said: the conclusion is that there are two corrected positions, and the relied-upon is the necessity of making it up because it is the apparent transmission and the texts.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement listing invalidators (mufsidat) of fasting. The author enumerates items 49-57 of the book. These actions break the fast: (49) inserting an object into the rectum, (50) similar into the vagina for women, (51) deliberately inhaling smoke, (52) inducing vomiting—according to the Zahir al-Riwayah even if less than a mouthful invalidates, but Abu Yusuf required a mouthful, and this is the correct view; (53) swallowing vomit that was brought up and is a mouthful while remembering one's fast; (54) eating food stuck between teeth if it is the size of a chickpea; (55) making the intention to fast during the day after having eaten forgetfully before making the intention—this invalidates because the intention came after a nullifying action; (56) losing consciousness—even for the entire month, but the day it began or the night it began is not made up; (57) non-continuous insanity for the entire month—the correct view is that no make-up is required, but there is a dispute: the footnote from Ibn Abidin indicates the relied-upon view is that make-up is necessary because it is the Zahir al-Riwayah. Students should note the nuance: for consciousness-related invalidators, the ruling distinguishes between the onset day and subsequent days.

Key Terms
دبر (dubur): The anus; insertion of an object into the rectum invalidates fasting.
استقاء (istighā’): Inducing vomiting deliberately. If it reaches a mouthful and is returned, it invalidates.
إغماء (ighmā’): Unconsciousness. If it occurs during the day, the fast of that day is not valid, but the day of onset or the night is not made up.
ملء الفم (mil’ al-fam): A mouthful; used as a measure for vomiting and food left between teeth. Approximately the amount that fills the mouth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what amount of induced vomiting invalidates the fast?
A) Any amount, even less than a mouthful.
B) Only a mouthful or more.
C) Only if the vomit is returned.
D) Only if the vomiting is unintentional.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has nuanced positions on fasting invalidators; the relied-upon views often resolve internal disputes, such as requiring a mouthful for vomiting and qada for insanity.

Obligation to Refrain After Invalid Fast (Imsak)
Ruling statement listing those obliged to refrain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four categories of people who must refrain (imsak) for the rest of the day in Ramadan and explain the ruling for each.

Core Idea
Even when the fast is invalid, the sanctity of Ramadan demands refraining for the remaining day.

In Ramadan, the sacred time demands respect even for a broken fast. This passage – a memorization anchor – lists exactly who must stop eating and drinking for the rest of the day. Understanding these categories ensures you know when imsak is obligatory (wajib) and when qada is also required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on 'imsāk' – refraining from fast-breaking actions for the rest of the day. The ruling is 'wajib' (obligatory) for specific persons.
2
List the four categories
Memorize the four: (1) one whose fast is invalidated, (2) menstruating/postpartum woman purified after dawn, (3) child reaching puberty during the day, (4) disbeliever embracing Islam after dawn.
3
Note the exception for qada
All except the child and the convert must also make up the fast (qada). The child and convert were not obligated at dawn, so no make-up is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [ فيمن يجب عليه الإمساك بقية اليوم] يجب الإمساك بقية اليوم على : 1 - مَن فَسَدَ صومه (٢). 2- وعلى حائض، ونُفَسَاء طَهُرَتا بعد طلوع الفجر (٣). 3- وعلى صبي بلغ. 4- وكافر أسلم بعد الطلوع. وعليهم القضاء، إلا الأخيرين (٤) (١) وقيل يستحب مراقي والاستحباب هو مذهب المالكية والشافعية ورواية أحمد ينظر الموسوعة الفقهية الكويتية ٨٠/٢٨ عن (٢) وكذلك مسافر أقام. مراقي. (٣) لاحتمال أنهما طهرتا قبل الفجر. (٤) أي الصبي والكافر؛ لعدم الخطاب عند طلوع الفجر عليهما.
Chapter: On who must refrain for the rest of the day. Refraining for the rest of the day is obligatory on: 1. One whose fast has become invalid. 2. A menstruating woman and a postpartum woman who become purified after dawn. 3. A child who reaches puberty. 4. A disbeliever who embraces Islam after dawn. And they must make up (the fast), except the last two. (1) It was said: recommended (mustahabb) - Muraqi; and recommendation is the madhhab of Malikis, Shafi'is, and a narration of Ahmad; see Kuwaiti Fiqh Encyclopedia 28/80. (2) Likewise a traveler who takes up residence - Muraqi. (3) Due to the possibility that they became purified before dawn. (4) i.e., the child and the disbeliever; because there was no address (obligation) upon them at dawn.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the chapter on fasting. It specifies who must refrain from eating, drinking, and other fast-breakers for the remaining part of the day. There are four categories: (1) anyone whose fast has been invalidated (e.g., by accidental eating, though they must still refrain); (2) a menstruating woman or a woman in postnatal bleeding who becomes pure after dawn—they must refrain out of respect for the time, although their fast is invalid; (3) a child who reaches puberty during the day—they become obliged to fast from that point; (4) a disbeliever who accepts Islam after dawn. All four must make up the fast (qada) except the last two (child and convert), because they were not obligated at dawn (subh sadiq). The footnotes add that some scholars consider the refraining as mustahabb rather than wajib, and that a traveler who takes up residence after dawn is similar to one whose fast is invalid. This ruling emphasizes the sanctity of the daytime of Ramadan even for those whose fast is invalid or who become obligated mid-day.

Key Terms
إمساك (imsāk): Refraining from eating, drinking, and other acts that break the fast for the remaining part of the day.
فسد صومه (fasada sawmuhu): One whose fast has become invalid, e.g., due to forgetting and eating, or other invalidator that requires qada.
صبي بلغ (ṣabī balagha): A minor who reaches puberty during the day; they become obligated to fast from that moment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman who was menstruating becomes pure after dawn (fajr) in Ramadan. What is her obligation according to the text?
A) She must refrain for the rest of the day and make up the fast later.
B) She must refrain but does not have to make up the fast.
C) She may eat and drink because her fast is not valid.
D) She must only make up the fast without refraining.

Key Takeaway
In Ramadan, even after a fast is invalidated, the day's sanctity demands refraining (imsak) as a wajib act, and only those who were obligated at dawn must make up the fast.

Seven Disliked Acts for the Fasting Person
List of seven disliked acts with a footnote on karaha type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven disliked acts for a fasting person and distinguish between the views of Ibn Abidin (tanzih) and al-Tahtawi (tahrim) regarding their legal weight.

Core Idea
Seven specific acts are disliked for the fasting person, but whether they are sinful or just offensive depends on the scholar's view.

The matn of Nur al-Idah lists seven acts that are considered makruh for someone observing a fast. These include tasting food, chewing gum, kissing, and even collecting saliva. A footnote by later Hanafi authorities reveals a subtle difference of opinion: Ibn Abidin considers them merely offensive (tanzih), while al-Tahtawi considers them prohibitively disliked (tahrim). This lesson will help you memorize the list and understand the practical difference in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the list of seven acts
Read through the seven items carefully. Note that item 5 includes a condition: it is disliked only if one fears ejaculation or intercourse.
The list is a memorization anchor for Hanafi fiqh.
2
Understand the two views on karaha
From the footnote, identify that Ibn Abidin said the dislike is tanzihi, while al-Tahtawi said tahrimi. Consider the practical implication: if tahrimi, the act is sinful and should be avoided; if tanzihi, it is better to avoid but not sinful.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf among later scholars.
3
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Imagine a fasting person chews gum that does not dissolve. Apply the ruling: it is disliked. Then consider which view you follow: if you follow Ibn Abidin, it's offensive; if Tahtawi, it's sinful.
The mufta bihi is generally tanzih, but caution is advised.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يكره للصائم : و(1) كره للصائم سبعة أشياء:
١ - ذوق شيء.
٢ - ومضعه بلا عذر.
٣ - ومضغ العلك(2).
٤ - والقبلة.
٥ - والمباشرة إن لم يأمن فيهما على نفسه الإنزال، أو الجماع، في ظاهر الرواية.
٦ - وجمع الريق في الفم، ثم ابتلاعه.
٧ - وما ظن أنه يضعفه كالفصد، والحجامة.
(1) قال ابن عابدين ٣٢٩/٦: الظاهر أن الكراهة في هذه الأشياء تنزيهية. اهـ أما الطحطاوي في حاشيته على المراقي ص ٥٥٩ فقال: الظاهر أنها تحريمية.
(2) الذي لا يصل منه شيء إلى الجوف مع الريق. مراقي.
Disliked for the fasting person: And (1) seven things are disliked for the fasting person: 1. Tasting something. 2. Chewing it without an excuse. 3. Chewing gum (2). 4. The kiss. 5. Direct intimate contact if he does not feel safe from ejaculation or intercourse that day, according to the apparent narration. 6. Collecting saliva in the mouth and then swallowing it. 7. What he thinks will weaken him, like phlebotomy and cupping. (1) Ibn Abidin said: Apparently the dislike in these things is tanzihi (offensive). End quote. As for al-Tahtawi in his gloss on al-Maraqi, p. 559, he said: Apparently it is tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). (2) That which does not reach the belly with the saliva. Maraqui.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage lists seven acts that are disliked (makruh) for a fasting person. The author, al-Shurunbulali, presents them without explicit proof, as this is a matn (core text) meant for memorization. The footnote by Ibn Abidin clarifies that the dislike is tanzihi (offensive, not sinful), while al-Tahtawi holds it is tahrimi (prohibitively disliked). The student should note that item 5 includes a condition: it is disliked only if one fears ejaculation or intercourse; otherwise, it may be permissible. The ruling on gum chewing applies only to gum that yields no substance to the stomach.

Key Terms
كَرِهَ (kariha): To be disliked; in this text, it means the act is disapproved but the ruling is qualified by the footnote as either tanzih or tahrim.
العلك (al-‘ilk): Chewing gum; specifically gum that does not dissolve or reach the stomach.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Ibn Abidin
B) Al-Tahtawi
C) Al-Shurunbulali
D) Muhammad al-Shaybani

Key Takeaway
The seven acts are disliked, but the dominant Hanafi fatwa considers them makruh tanzih, not sinful.

The Recommended Acts (Mustahabbat) of Fasting
Lists three recommended acts for the fasting person, though the second act is implied.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three recommended acts for the fasting person and explain the textual ambiguity in the passage.

Core Idea
Suhur, delaying suhur, and breaking fast early are three recommended acts for the fasting person.

This passage from Nur al-Idah lists three mustahabbat (recommended) acts for the fasting person. However, the text as it appears combines two items under the first number, leading to a common misunderstanding. Let's clarify the actual three acts and their legal status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stated number of recommended acts
The text explicitly says 'three things' (ثلاثة أشياء), but the subsequent enumeration shows only two numbers. This indicates a textual compression where the first item combines two acts.
The three acts are: (1) suhur and breaking fast early (combined), (2) delaying suhur. The intended three are suhur, delaying suhur, and breaking fast early.
2
Distinguish the three acts
Break down the combined first number: 'السحور وتعجيل الفطر' means both suhur and ta'jil al-fitr. The second number 'وتأخيره' refers to delaying suhur. Thus the three are: suhur, delaying suhur, and breaking fast early.
All three are mustahab (recommended).
3
Understand the condition for ta'jil al-fitr
The text specifies 'في غير يوم غيم' (on a non-cloudy day). This means breaking fast early is recommended only when sunset can be reliably observed. On a cloudy day, it is not recommended to break immediately because of uncertainty.
This precaution avoids breaking before actual sunset.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يستحب للصائم : ويستحب له ثلاثة أشياء: ١ - السحور وتعجيل الفطر في غير يوم غيم. ٢ - وتأخيره.
What is recommended for the fasting person: And three things are recommended for him: 1. The pre-dawn meal and breaking the fast early on a non-cloudy day. 2. And delaying it.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage lists recommended acts (mustahabbat) for the fasting person. The first item combines the pre-dawn meal (suhur) and breaking the fast early (ta'jil al-fitr) on a clear day. The second item is 'delaying it' (takhir), which refers to delaying the suhur. Thus the three are: suhur, delaying suhur, and breaking fast early. The omission of a separate number for 'ta'jil al-fitr' is a textual ambiguity; the intended meaning is that these three are all recommended.

Key Terms
سحور (suhur): The pre-dawn meal before fasting; it is recommended and a sunnah.
تعجيل الفطر (ta'jil al-fitr): Breaking the fast as soon as sunset is confirmed, without delay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
The fasting person is recommended to perform suhur (pre-dawn meal), delay it, and break the fast early on a clear day.

Permissible and Recommended Acts for the Fasting Person
List of permissible and recommended actions during fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the nine actions not disliked for a fasting person and the three recommended acts, distinguishing between them and applying the condition of safety for physical contact.

Core Idea
Nine acts are not disliked; three are recommended — a clear Hanafi guide to permissible and encouraged actions during fasting.

This passage from Nur al-Idah provides a practical list for the fasting person, clarifying common misunderstandings. It explains that actions like kissing, applying kohl, and using a wet toothstick do not harm the fast, and it highlights the sunnah of suhur and hastening iftar. The list is a useful reference for daily practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify two categories
Recognize that the passage divides actions into two groups: nine that are not disliked (makruh) and three that are recommended (mustahabb).
2
List the nine non-disliked acts
Memorize the nine items in order: kissing/touching with safety from desire, oiling mustache, applying kohl, cupping that does not weaken, bloodletting, using siwak (even wet), rinsing mouth/nose (if not for wudu), bathing, and wrapping in a wet cloth to cool down.
The footnote adds that lascivious touching is disliked even if safe from emission.
3
Apply condition for physical contact
Note that kissing and touching are only permissible if one is safe from desire. Lascivious touching is disliked.
The condition is 'ma'a al-amn' (with safety).
4
Understand the ruling on siwak
Siwak is sunnah at any time during the fast, even at the end of the day, and even if wet. It does not break the fast.
This differs from some schools that dislike siwak after midday.
5
Apply the three recommended acts
Take suhur and delay it until just before fajr; hasten to break the fast on non-cloudy days.
On cloudy days, one may wait to be certain of maghrib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما لا يكره للصائم : وتسعة أشياء لا تكره للصائم: ۱ - القُبْلَةُ، والمباشرة مع الأمن) ٢ - ودَهْنُ الشارب. والاكتحال. ٤- والحجامة التي لا تضعفه. ه والقصد ٦ - والسواك آخر النهار ، بل هو سنة كأوله، ولو كان رطباً، أو مبلُولاً بالماء. والمضمضة ، والاستنشاق لغير وضوء. والاغتسال. ٩- والتلقف بثوب مُبتل للتبرد ، على المفتى به. [ما يُستحب للصائم : ] ويُستحب له ثلاثة أشياء: ١ - السحور وتأخيره. ٢- وتعجيل الفطر في غير يومٍ غَيْمٍ. ***** (۱) نقل ط على المراقي ص ٥٦٠ عن الهندية أن المباشرة الفاحشة تكره وإن أمن من الإنزال أو الجماع، هو الصحيح، وعن المحيط أنه لا خلاف في ذلك.
Things not disliked for the fasting person: And nine things are not disliked for the fasting person: 1 - The kiss and touching with safety. 2 - Oiling the mustache and applying kohl. 4 - Cupping that does not weaken him. 5 - And cupping(?)/bloodletting. 6 - Using the toothstick at the end of the day, rather it is sunnah like at its beginning, even if it is wet or moistened with water. And rinsing the mouth and sniffing water for other than ablution. And bathing. 9 - Wrapping oneself in a wet cloth to cool down, according to the relied-upon opinion. [What is recommended for the fasting person:] And three things are recommended for him: 1 - The pre-dawn meal (suhur) and delaying it. 2 - Hastening to break the fast on a day that is not cloudy. ***** (1) Narrated from al-Hindiya that lascivious touching is disliked even if one is safe from emission or intercourse; that is the correct view, and from al-Muhit that there is no disagreement on that.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement categorizing actions during fasting into non-disliked and recommended. The author lists nine actions that are not makruh for the fasting person: kissing and touching one's spouse without fear of arousal (non-lascivious), oiling the mustache, applying kohl, cupping that does not weaken, bloodletting (likely intended by 'al-qasd'), using the toothstick at any time (even at the end of the day, which is sunnah), rinsing the mouth and sniffing water if not for ablution, bathing, and wrapping in a wet cloth to cool down. The footnote clarifies that lascivious touching is disliked even if safe from emission. Then three recommended acts are stated: taking the pre-dawn meal (suhur) and delaying it, and hastening to break the fast on non-cloudy days.

Key Terms
القبلة (al-qubla): The kiss between spouses during fasting, which is not disliked if one is safe from desire.
المباشرة (al-mubashara): Touching skin-to-skin between spouses during fasting, not disliked if safe from arousal.
السواك (al-siwak): Using a toothstick; sunnah at any time during the fast, even wet.
التلقف (al-talaquf): Wrapping oneself in a wet cloth to cool down; not disliked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT listed among the nine things not disliked for the fasting person?
A) Kissing with safety
B) Applying kohl
C) Using a toothstick at the end of the day
D) Swallowing food intentionally

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits many actions during fasting that are often misunderstood, while emphasizing the sunnah of suhur and hastening iftar.
